evaporation organic Rankine cycle for small-
scale solar thermal applications

DTU Mechanical Engineering

Project Details

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
The general concerns regarding environmental and sustainability issues have increased drastically
over the past years. The eﬀort to produce cheap and renewable energy has risen as a result of
this. This eﬀort also includes decentralised locations, where large power plants are not necessary.
The organic Rankine cycle (ORC) is an eﬃcient way to use heat for power generation. Numerous
literature study the ORC architecture in attempts to improve its performance and one of these
modiﬁed cycles is the partial evaporation organic Rankine cycle (PEORC). The general advantage
of using a PEORC in comparison to a conventional subcritical organic Rankine cycle (SCORC)
is a better match of the temperature proﬁles for the heat source and working ﬂuid. This leads
to an increase in exergy eﬃciency and thereby better usage of the heat.
Furthermore, studies
show that the PEORC has a higher heat input but also a higher net output, thus increasing the
investment cost and revenue. Literature suggests that the greatest advantages of the PEORC occur
at low temperature heat sources and plants with high full load hours, such as geothermal power
plants. However, not all locations have the possibility to have such plants. Therefore, this study
presents a techno-economic performance of the solar integrated partial evaporation organic Rankine
cycle, located in Antofagasta, Chile. The study uses micro-structured foil-based concentrated solar
power (CSP) collectors for small- to medium-scale solar thermal applications, with a ﬁxed gross
power output of 1 MWe and a medium-temperature heat source. Three diﬀerent thermal energy
storage (TES) technologies, Therminol 55 and rapeseed oil as heat transfer ﬂuids (HTF) and various
working ﬂuids, were examined. Both power generation only and cogeneration conﬁgurations were
evaluated.
For the cogeneration conﬁgurations freshwater was produced through a multi-eﬀect
distillation (MED) system. For the case study it was found that the PEORC was never able to
outperform the SCORC for both power only generation and cogeneration conﬁgurations. However,
cogeneration showed to increase the performance for most of the trilateral cycles and decrease the
performance for all of the subcritical cycles.

==PAGE:17==
1
Introduction
This section provides background information about organic Rankine cycles, the objective of the
thesis and a brief literature review.
1.1
Background and Motivation
Due to the increasing amount of carbon dioxide in the atmosphere a large number of environmental
problems, such as global warming, have emerged. The ﬁrst international attempt to reduce the
pollution of greenhouse gases happened in 1997 where the Kyoto-protocol was adopted and then
eight years later entered into force [8]. However, due to a continuous increase of greenhouse gases
released in the atmosphere after 2005 this treaty was replaced and enhanced in 2015 by the Paris
Agreement. The goal of this agreement is to avoid dangerous climate change by limiting global
warming to below 2°C, preferably 1.5°C, compared to pre-industrial levels [7].
According to a
report from the International Renewable Energy Agency (IRENA) in 2017 two-thirds of global
greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions stem from the production and use of energy [21]. This makes
energy decarbonisation essential in realising the goals of the Paris Agreement and it requires raising
the share of renewable energy sources signiﬁcantly.
Furthermore, this energy transition has to
be economically feasible if investors and companies are to capitalise on the many opportunities
associated with the use of renewable technologies. It is important to implement appropriate cost
eﬀective power generation technologies in all regions of the world. A vast used technology is the
organic Rankine cycle (ORC), which can produce electricity by utilizing heat from e.g. thermal
solar panels, making it a valid green alternative to fossil fuel power plants. Additionally, the ORC
allows electricity generation at low capacity and low cost, making it ideal for decentralized locations
[69]. For that reason, the optimization of this application can beneﬁt many places worldwide, while
also being on track to meet the goals of the Paris agreement. Several literature [65][66] report
Chile, more speciﬁcally in the Atacama desert region, to be the best location in the world for solar
energy, due to its extremely dry climate, high amount of solar hours and concentration of solar
irradiance [67][68].
This thesis takes case study of concentrated solar power (CSP) integrated into an ORC system to
convert harvested heat into thermal power. Thermal energy storages (TES) have been proven to be
signiﬁcantly more eﬃcient and reliable than battery storages, hence using solar thermal application
instead of solar photovoltaics. Existing solar ORC systems are usually subcritical organic Rankine
cycle (SCORC) based, but various cycle architectures exist for the ORC. The diﬀerence between
the SCORC and the partial evaporation Ranine cycle (PEORC) is the evaporation quality of the
working ﬂuid in the evaporator. Studies show that the PEORC has a higher exergy eﬃciency than
the SCORC, due to a better temperature proﬁle match between the heat source and the working
ﬂuid. The trilateral organic Rankine cycle (TLORC) is another modiﬁed ORC, where the quality
1
==PAGE:18==
1.2
Literature Review
of the working ﬂuid is zero. From a study by Hays et al. [76] it was shown that the pinch point
temperature diﬀerence for an ORC restricts the amount of heat transfer in the evaporator. The
results from this study showed that the TLORC could reach a 27% increase in heat input and 15%
increase in net power output compared to a traditional ORC due to the removal of the pinch point
limitation. The investment cost of the solar collector ﬁeld and power block is higher for TLORCs
compared to SCORCs. When Pgross = 1 MW for both cycles, a signiﬁcant amount of the heat
input to the TLORC is wasted in the condenser, instead of being utilized as a revenue. Therefore,
it may be very advantageous for the TLORC in this work to operate as a cogeneration plant. The
revenue for the TLORCs will be higher because of the higher heat release to the heat sink due to
its lower thermal eﬃciency.
The PEORC requires expanders speciﬁcally designed to handle two-phase ﬂows, since the two-
phase mixture can signiﬁcantly damage the blades in traditional single-phase turbines. Moreover,
the research on two-phase turbines is still at early level and very little information is publicly
available about the very few commercially available solutions.
1.2
Literature Review
There has been performed an extensive amount of studies on electricity and freshwater generation
for solar integrated SCORC with medium temperature, however most literature for PEORCs con-
sider low temperature heat sources. In a study by Iqbal et al. [92] for a low-temperature heat
source, the TLORC showed to have a 50% higher power generation than the SCORC, using the
same heat source conditions. Iqbal et al. additionally found that the TLORC was feasible at lower
temperature heat sources than the SCORC. Furthermore, Fischer [99] showed that the exergy ef-
ﬁciency for the trilateral cycle decreases with an increase in heat source temperature. Hays et al.
[76] found that for low-grade heat the TLORC had a higher heat input, and therefore also cost.
However, it was also found that the TLORC had a higher net electricity output, and therefore also
higher revenue. In a study by Astolﬁet al. [86] where a 5 MW solar photovoltaic (PV) panel
system was compared with a CSP coupled SCORC system, in which it was concluded that as a
consequence of the intermittent nature of solar energy the CSP was superior due to thermal storage
being cheaper than the battery storage. Furthermore, reverse osmosis (RO) and a multi-eﬀect dis-
tillation (MED) system were investigated and compared in addition to the ORC system, in which
MED resulted in the most eﬀective integration. I. Baniasad et al. [85] also suggested that a MED
system for freshwater production is the least energy consumptive freshwater generation method,
and found a levelized cost of electricity (LCOE) and levelized cost of water (LCOW) of 0,16 €/kWh
and 1,38 €/m3, respectively. The study concluded that linear fresnel collectors with an indirect
solar thermal storage using molten salt with a capacity of 7.5 hours resulted in the lowest LCOE
and LCOW. Nafey et al. [87] studied a combined solar ORC with RO desalination using various
solar collectors and working ﬂuids, concluding a LCOW of 0,76 €/m3 using parabolic through col-
2
==PAGE:19==
1.3
Scope and Objectives
lectors (PTC) with toluene as working ﬂuid. In another study by Nafey et al. [88] a PTC solar
integrated SCORC was studied in an attempt to generate the cheapest fresh water from diﬀerent
solar thermal-powered recovery units for RO desalination resulting in LCOW values between 0,5
to 0,75 €/m3.
1.3
Scope and Objectives
The objective of the thesis is to perform a techno-economic evaluation of a partial evaporation
organic Rankine cycle in comparison to a subcritical organic Rankine cycle for small-to-medium
scale solar thermal applications. A ﬁxed gross electricity output from the generator of 1 MW is
assumed, and the objective is therefore to ﬁnd the cheapest conﬁguration to produce 1 MWe. Several
optimization variables, working ﬂuids and three diﬀerent thermal energy storage technologies are
investigated. In that relation optimal design conditions for both the PEORC and SCORC are
found and compared. Furthermore, the feasibility and performance of cogeneration is evaluated
and reported, of which the LCOE, LCOW and pay-back time (PBT) are used as main performance
comparison parameters.
The main purpose of the thesis is to evaluate and optimise the ORCs and hence having less focus on
other components such as the solar collector ﬁeld technology and economic analysis. The economic
analysis is evaluated from a technical and feasibility point of view, and does not include parameters
such as taxes, distribution and permits.
3
==PAGE:20==
2
Methodology
The following section presents the system description and methodologies used for the thermody-
namic cycle, optimization code and economic calculations.
2.1
System Description
The collector type used is concentrated medium temperature micro-structured polymer foil-based
collectors.
The collectors provide heat to the power blocks, where SCORCs and PEORCs are
evaluated. The heat transfer ﬂuid (HTF) from the solar circuit, Therminol 55 or rapeseed oil,
work for low-medium temperature solar collectors [12][70] and supply heat to the ORC through the
evaporator, which then expands the working ﬂuid in the turbine and generates electricity through
a generator. For SCORC, at the turbine outlet the working ﬂuid enters a recuperator cooling down
the exhaust vapour whilst preheating the opposing liquid before entering the evaporator. Seawater
is used to condense the working ﬂuid before entering the power circuit pump. The power output of
1 MWe ( ˙Pe) is assumed from the generator, the net electricity output ( ˙Pnet) is naturally lower due
to the consumption from various pumps. Three storage methods are compared: packed-bedrock
storage, 2T-direct storage and 2T-indirect storage.
Figure 1 illustrates a thermal solar collector ORC with an indirect TES. An internal heat exchanger
is required when using indirect storage, as molten salt (Hitec XL) is used in the indirect tank circuit.
Molten salt can be advantageous to use, as it is considerably cheaper than Therminol 55 [15][12],
however the freezing temperature is high [20] and hence a minimum temperature of 165 °is assumed
in the solar circuit for indirect storage. Rapeseed oil is not investigated for indirect TES, as the
cost of rapeseed oil is lower than molten salt, and thereby eliminating the advantage of using a
cheaper storage ﬂuid in an indirect storage conﬁguration.
4
==PAGE:21==
2.1
System Description
Figure 1: Solar integrated organic Rankine cycle with indirect thermal energy storage.
Figure 2 illustrates the system with a direct TES. The HTF is used directly as storage and therefore
no internal heat exchanger is required, which increases the heat transfer ﬂuid price, but decreases
component cost. Furthermore, the minimum temperature of the HTF can be signiﬁcantly lower
and thereby have a larger temperature span.
5
==PAGE:22==
2.1
System Description
Figure 2: Solar integrated organic Rankine cycle with direct thermal energy storage.
Figure 3 illustrates the system with packed-bed rock TES. The HTF is used to heat up the packed-
bed rock. Using this kind of storage can be advantageous because the rocks are cheap [22] and
no internal heat exchanger is required. Similar to direct storage, the minimum temperature of the
HTF can be signiﬁcantly lower than using indirect storage.
6
==PAGE:23==
2.1
System Description
Figure 3: Solar integrated organic Rankine cycle with packed-bed rock thermal energy storage.
The storages are used to increase the full load hours of the plants and thereby increase the capacity
factor of the systems.
A higher storage capacity results in a higher capacity factor but also a
higher cost. In this evaluation 8 hours of storage capacity is assumed. U. Herrmann et al. [56]
reported that a 6 hour and a 12 hour capacity TES system has a capacity factor on 41% and
55%, respectively. Therefore, a capacity factor on 45% is initially assumed. Energy storages are
used to minimize the supply and demand mismatch that will occur, due to varying direct normal
irradiance (DNI), ambient temperature (Ta) and electricity demand (Pe). However, DNI, Ta and
Pe will remain constant throughout the project.
2.1.1
Subcritical Cycle
In a conventional SCORC the heat recovery in the evaporator is limited due to the behavior of
the temperature proﬁles in the heat exchange process. The minimum temperature diﬀerence of the
working ﬂuid and heat source is the pinch point temperature. On one hand it is desirable to reduce
the pinch point temperature as much as possible to maximize the heat gain, but on the other hand
it happens at the expense of additional complexity and investment cost. Generally, a small pinch
point temperature leads to an eﬃcient but expensive heat transfer process, because the capital cost
increases due to larger heat exchangers are required. A large pinch point temperature results in
7
==PAGE:24==
2.1
System Description
poor heat exchange curves, especially when one moves away from the pinch point region and the
performance of the plant will thus be aﬀected greatly. For an economic optimum the pinch point
temperature should ﬁnd a trade-oﬀbetween having a higher capital cost versus higher eﬃciency,
and in general this optimum value is approximately 5 to 10 °C [91].
From the 2nd law of thermodynamics the entropy generation Sgen is deﬁned as:
Sgen =
˙Q
TL
−
˙Q
TH
=
˙Q · (TH −TL)
(TL · TH)
(1)
where ˙Q is the heat transferred from a high temperature TH, to a low temperature TL. Therefore,
a low temperature drop from TH to TL results in a low entropy generation thus increasing the
eﬃciency of the heat process. A subcritical cycle typically has a higher thermal eﬃciency than a
partial evaporation cycle [6].
In Figure 4 a typical T-s diagram of a subcritical cycle with a recuperator is sketched and the
pinch point temperatures (dTpp and dTppk) are illustrated. The evaporator will hence include a
preheater, evaporator and superheater.
Figure 4: Typical T,s-diagram for subcritical organic Rankine cycles..
2.1.2
Partial Evaporating Cycle
The main diﬀerence between PEORCs and SCORCs is the state of which the working ﬂuid is
entering the expander [6]. The working ﬂuid in the PEORC evaporator is heated to the two-phase
state before entering the expander leading to the occurrance of a wet expansion. This cycle presents
8
==PAGE:25==
2.1
System Description
a higher exergy eﬃciency [41] than the subcritical cycle because the temperature proﬁles in the
evaporator have a better match. This is depicted in Figure 5. However, PEORCs also require more
heat input and have higher working ﬂuid mass ﬂow rates because of the wet expansion, leading
to overall larger components. The expander also has to be capable of operating two-phase ﬂows
and that may limit the expander options. The PEORC evaporator consists of a preheater and
evaporator.
Figure 5: Typical T,s-diagram for partial evaporating organic Rankine cycles..
2.1.3
Trilateral Cycle
The TLORC is a type of partial evaporation that removes the pinch point temperature limitation
that occurs in a subcritical cycle. The working ﬂuid enters the expander as saturated liquid causing
the temperature proﬁles in the evaporator to run parallel to each other and the temperature dif-
ference is therefore almost constant during the entire heat exchange. This will reduce the entropy
generation signiﬁcantly and it will hence maximize the available heat for power production. The
TLORC evaporator consists of only a preheater and in Figure 6 a typical T-s diagram of a trilateral
cycle is illustrated. The cycle within the liquid-vapor dome appears to be triangular, hence giving
it the name trilateral cycle.
9
==PAGE:26==
2.1
System Description
Figure 6: Typical T,s-diagram for trilateral organic Rankine cycles..
2.1.4
Optimization Code
The models of the ORCs are developed in MATLAB, where the thermodynamic cycle is performed,
in which 6 decision variables are varied and optimized to ﬁnd the cheapest conﬁguration that
provides 1 MW gross electricity output. In Figure 7 a workﬂow diagram of the present work is
illustrated. The solar ﬁeld, and its respective three diﬀerent thermal energy storages are done by the
authors in MATLAB. The optimization code [49], developed by Roberto Pili (Postdoc. Researcher)
and later modiﬁed by Carlotta Tammone (Ph.d.
Student), was designed for low temperature
geothermal applications, and optimized for maximal power output with a ﬁxed heat input. The code
was then modiﬁed by the authors to be solar integrated with thermal energy storages, optimized for
capital investment cost with a ﬁxed power output and thus minimized heat input. Additionally, the
air condensed cooling fan has been replaced with a water cooling pump and cogeneration has been
implemented. Moreover, an axial-ﬂow turbine eﬃciency script was also added to the code, which
Sigurd Indrehus, a fellow Master thesis student in cooperation with C. Tammone, has developed.
10
==PAGE:27==
2.1
System Description
Figure 7: Workﬂow diagram of the present work.
The code has 6 optimization variables that can vary from lower to upper bound in order to ﬁnd
the cheapest conﬁguration. The 6 variables are: evaporation pressure (Pwf,eva), quality of working
ﬂuid leaving the evaporator (xouteva), recuperator eﬀectiveness (receff), condensing temperature
(Twf,k) and pinch point temperature in the evaporator (∆Tpp) and condenser (∆Tppk).
The
boundaries of the optimization variables are presented later in Table 3. The optimization routine
is based on the particle swarm algorithm. The aim of the optimization routine is to ﬁnd the set of
design variables characterizing the system that gives the optimum value of the objective function.
In the present study the optimization function is to minimize the capital investment cost and
levelized cost of water for power generation only and cogeneration conﬁgurations, respectively. A
ﬂowchart of the computational method is represented in Figure 8. The algorithm guesses on values
between the lower and upper bound for the 6 optimization variables, which are used to calculate the
thermodynamic states. These are then used to calculate various parameters, costs and ultimately
the optimization goal. The algorithm then remembers the value for the optimization goal and
compares it with other values of the optimization goal. This iterative process continues until a
deﬁnite minimum (or maximum) has been found. A visual representation can be seen in Figure 8.
11
==PAGE:28==
2.1
System Description
Figure 8: Flowchart of the optimization code.
The code has been designed so that when a constraint is violated, due to possible interfering guess
values that may overlap, then the optimization goal equates to an infeasible high number. Thus, the
algorithm knows that it should not guess on that combination of values of optimization variables
again. The MATLAB code for one of the conﬁgurations is seen in Appendix B.4

solar ﬁeld is either Therminol 55 or rapeseed oil. Hitec XL is used as storage ﬂuid for the indirect
storage. The data for the heat exchangers is not listed in Table 1 but will appear later. These
parameters will remain constant during all calculations if nothing else is indicated.

evaporator outlet heat source temperature is 30 °C for direct and bedrock storage, and 165 °C
for the indirect storage. In addition with the minimum 5 °C pinch point temperature and a 10%
13

safety margin, the lower bound is determined for the evaporation pressure. The upper bound of
the evaporation pressure ensures that the working ﬂuid never reaches its critical pressure. The
pinch point temperatures boundaries are based on literature [91]. The recuperator eﬀectiveness
is based on literature [103] and heat transfer theory. The pinch point temperature diﬀerence in
the recuperator is included in the eﬀectiveness. The working ﬂuid evaporator quality varies from
1 to 2 for the SCORC. This is purely for computational reasons, as the quality cannot exceed 1.
The minimum condensation temperature is based on the cold source inlet temperature and the
minimum condenser pinch point temperature. The maximum condensing temperature is based on
the boiling point of water at atmospheric pressure.

2.1.6
Working Fluids
The possibility of selecting the appropriate working ﬂuid is a very signiﬁcant degree of freedom
that has to be examined, to make sure the suitable working ﬂuid is used. Choosing the appropriate
working ﬂuid will permit proper volume ﬂow rates, which is necessary for optimum expander size,
and will also determine evaporation and condensation pressure and enthalpy drop during expansion.
Therefore, the working ﬂuid has a tremendous inﬂuence on the system eﬃciency and cost [34].
The primary choice of working ﬂuid is determined by the heat source temperature. Mago et al.
[39] concluded that ﬂuids with higher critical temperatures give higher eﬃciencies, as a high critical
temperature permits higher turbine inlet temperature to expand to lower pressure at the turbine
exit, and thus a larger enthalpy diﬀerence delivered to generate power.
However, the critical
temperature and density also inﬂuence the speciﬁc vapor density, which has a high inﬂuence on
the system design. The high inﬂuence from vapor density is because a high density results in a
low condensing pressure and small expander size, due to the deﬁnition of the size parameter (SP)
deﬁned in Eq.(5). The volume in the equation is a product of the density and a high density results
14
==PAGE:31==
2.1
System Description
in a small SP and thus small turbine size. Moreover, ﬂuids with high latent heat and low speciﬁc
heat are typically good, because it increases the absorption of energy in the evaporator and increases
exergy eﬃciency and reduces pump work [38]. Similarly are ﬂuids with high thermal conductivities
related to a high heat transfer coeﬃcient, and hence requires a smaller heat exchanger area. In
addition, the working ﬂuid must be compatible with lubricants and metals from piping, expander
etc. as well as being stable, safe and environmentally accepted. Some ﬂuids, depending on their T-s
diagram slope and ORC type, may need superheating in the evaporator, in order not to condense
in the expander.
Predicting which working ﬂuid is most suitable can be diﬃcult before running simulations, as
there are conﬂicting conditions in the criteria, e.g. high molecular weights and compressibility of
working ﬂuids reduce the number of turbine stages and enhance the turbine eﬃciency [37]. Heavy
molecules cause small enthalpy drops during the expansion but also result in poor heat transfer
coeﬃcients [58]. High critical pressures require high heat transfer rates and heat exchanger areas,
hence increasing cost [36]. Therefore, the optimization algorithm ﬁnds many trade-oﬀs to optimize
the optimization goal.
The proposed working ﬂuids can be seen in Table 4. The values for the critical temperature (Tcrit)
and pressure (Pcrit) are found from the refprop library in MATLAB, and the molar mass (M) is
found from Engineering Equation Solver (EES). The global warming potential (GWP) and ozone
depletion potential (ODP) are found from references [61], [62], [63]. The critical temperature gives a
relative estimation of ORC cycle eﬃciency, as higher critical temperature ﬂuids often give a higher
cycle eﬃciency [40]. While the optimization code does not consider any environmental factors,
these must not be neglected. Therefore, GWP and ODP of the investigated working ﬂuids are
listed in Table 4. The value of GWP was not available for all working ﬂuids. GWP is a number
that refers to the potential global warming damage caused by a ﬂuid relatively to CO2 during a
100 years time period, where CO2 has a GWP of 1 [59]. ODP refers to a ﬂuids potential ability to
destroy the stratospheric ozone layer relatively to the ﬂuid R11 [59]. All working ﬂuids should have
an ODP of 0, as required by the Montreal Protocol [60]. None of the working ﬂuids are evaluated
to have a too high GWP or ODP to be used. The choice of investigated working ﬂuids are based
on several studies [15], [51], [85], [86], [87].

The thermodynamical state points and conditions for each component are explained in the following
section.
2.2.1
Expander
The maximum speciﬁc enthalpy drop across each turbine stage is limited at 65 kJ/kg, as suggested
by Astolﬁet al. [10].
From the electricity output of 1 MWe the mass ﬂow rate of the working ﬂuid can be estimated with
the following correlation:
˙mwf =
˙Pe · 1
ηg
houtwf,eva −houtwfe
(2)
where ˙Pe is the electricity output of the expander of 1 MWe, ηg is the eﬃciency of the generator,
houtwf,eva is the enthalpy of the working ﬂuid at the evaporator outlet and houtwf,e is the enthalpy
of the working ﬂuid at the turbine outlet.
The enthalpy at the expander outlet houtwf,e is calculated from the turbine inlet enthalpy, the
isentropic enthalpy at the turbine outlet and the turbine eﬃciency, estimated from an iterative
calculation loop.
houtwf,e = houtwf,eva −ηe · (houtwf,eva −houtwf,es)
(3)
where houtwf,e is the enthalpy at the expander outlet, houtwf,eva is the enthalpy at the evaporator
outlet and houtwf,es is the isentropic enthalpy at the expander outlet.
Axial-ﬂow turbines are used in approximately 90% of the worldwide energy production [9] and have
tremendous variations in application, rotational speed and eﬃciency. A SCORC with an axial-ﬂow
16
==PAGE:33==
2.2
Organic Rankine Cycle
turbine is considered while two other types of expanders are investigated for the PEORC. The
PEORC operates in the two-phase region in the evaporator, which can be advantageous because
wet expansion cycles present higher exergy eﬃciency than the subcritical cycles, due to better
matching of the temperature proﬁles in the evaporator [41][42]. This also means that a traditional
turbine expander, used for SCORC cycles, cannot be used for PE cycles, as wet steam can cause
damage to the rotor blades, hence a variable phase turbine and wet screw expander are evaluated
for the PEORC. Screw expanders are volumetric machines, and their eﬃciencies are more sensitive
to expansion pressure ratio than turbines and furthermore large screw expanders have a lower
eﬃciency than subcritical cycle turbine expanders [24]. The main disadvantage of screw expanders,
and in general of volumetric devices, is their small internal volume ratio which usually ranges
between 3 and 7, values which are much lower than those generally adopted in commercial ORCs.
Furthermore, the correlation of eﬃciency and the information regarding screw expanders are derived
by experimental campaigns on screw compressors in reverse operation [51]. The few companies
that produce screw expanders are very secretive towards their research and this may have a direct
impact on the results from this work. The variable phase turbine is another expander option that
can handle the wet steam in the PEORC. The information regarding this type of turbine is also
limited, and it will therefore be treated as a normal axial-ﬂow turbine.
The number of turbine stages and expander eﬃciency for axial-ﬂow turbines is calculated based on
the following correlation [10]:
ηe =
15
X
i=0
Ai · Fi
(4)
where Ai are coeﬃcients dependent on the number of turbine stages and Fi is a parameter dependent
on turbine size parameter (SPe) and isentropic volumetric ratio (V Re) [11]. The SPe and V Re are
found based on the following correlations:
SPe = Vout,is0.5
hout,is0.25
(5)
V Re = ρin,e
ρout,e
(6)
where Vout,is is the volumetric ﬂow rate during isentropic expansion, hout,is is the isentropic speciﬁc
enthalpy for the working ﬂuid at the turbine outlet, ρin,e is the density of the working ﬂuid at the
turbine inlet and ρout,e at the outlet.
The partial evaporation ORC requires an expander that can handle two-phase ﬂows hence making
the screw expander a plausible option. The volume ratio is limited to 5 pr. screw expander. When
the volume ratio for the ORC exceeds this limit, multiple screw expanders will be connected in
series [43]. Therefore, when the maximum volume ratio per each stage is 5, the number of stages
in series is calculated using Eq. (7).
17
==PAGE:34==

Organic Rankine Cycle
nstages,series = log(V Re)
log(5)
(7)
The volume coeﬃcient of the screw expander is deﬁned as the ratio of the outlet volumetric ﬂow
divided with the power output [42], which can be seen in Eq. (8).
V C =
˙Vout,e
˙We
(8)
The Volume Coeﬃcient for each screw expander should be within the range 0.25 to 0.6 m3/MJ
according to a screening of refrigeration and heat pump applications using a compressor [45].
For that reason the screw expanders in each stage of the series has to fulﬁll the volume coeﬃcient
requirement of 0.25 - 0.6 m3/MJ. Therefore, more screw expanders have to be parallelly connected
to the stages in series where the volume coeﬃcient exceeds 0.6 m3/MJ and larger devices are
required if it is less than 0.25 m3/MJ.
Electricity produced from the expander is assumed to be Pe =
1MW
ηg
which results in 1 MW
electricity after the generator. However, various pump consumptions must be subtracted to ﬁnd
the net electricity output, seen in Eq. (9).
Pnet = Pe
ηg
−Pp −Pp,hs −Pp,cs −Pp,FW
(9)
where Pe is the power output after the generator, ηg is the eﬃciency of the generator, Pp, Pp,hs,
Pp,cs and Pp,FW are the pump consumptions of the ORC circuit, heat source, cold source and
freshwater, respectively. If the plant is only producing power then Pp,FW = 0. The storage pump
consumptions are considered by a balancing cost in the storages.
2.2.2
Screw Expanders
The screw expanders are split into series and parallel connections, where a volume ratio (VR) of 5
[41] is used to determine the number of stages in series. A volume coeﬃcient (VC) between 0.25
to 0.6 m3
MJ pr. expander [42] is used to determine the number of stages in parallel, because the VR
already limits the volumetric ﬂow rate at the outlet of the expander. The TLORC with packed-bed
rock TES and cyclopentane as working ﬂuid requires 18 screw expanders combined in series and
parallel to be operational due to the VR and VC limitation. Furthermore, the cost of the screw
expanders for this speciﬁc conﬁguration is 4.7 M€ based on Astolﬁ’s cost correlations [51] while the
cost of the turbine for the same conﬁguration using a SCORC is only 0.39 M€. The high volume
ratio in the TLORC causes the price diﬀerence and the unfeasible amount of screw expanders. It
should also be noted that the volume ratio is expected to be high when using a TLORC, as the
density change from saturated liquid to a 2-phase liquid/vapor mixture is signiﬁcant. A volume ratio
18
==PAGE:35==
2.2
Organic Rankine Cycle
limit of 5 pr. expander appears to be very low for the conditions given at expander inlet and outlet.
This is supported by the cost correlation Eq. (83) as the initial cost of the screw expander is very
low, and it is primarily aﬀected by the volume ﬂow rate. Therefore, the use of screw expanders using
correlations from [51] for PEORCs and TLORCs with a heat source inlet temperature of 278°C and
power output of 1 MWe has shown to be infeasible. Diﬀerent results may occur if more appropriate
screw expander correlations for larger power outputs were accessible. However, due to secrecy and
patents by manufacturers there is little data available. Literature [41] [51] also conclude that the
minuscule data available may interfere with quality results, as appropriate correlations cannot be
obtained outside of companies. A variable phase turbine is instead used for PEORC and TLORC
architectures, assuming same eﬃciency correlations as for SCORC [11].
2.2.3
Recuperator
The working ﬂuid at the expander outlet enters the recuperator, in order to preheat the liquid
before the evaporator and thus increase the thermal eﬃciency [14], by recovering the waste heat of
the expander. The recuperator eﬀectiveness (receff) is the percentage of the actual heat transferred
compared to the maximum heat possible transferred. The recuperator is only used if it is necessary
to cool down the expander outlet ﬂuid or preheat the working ﬂuid at the evaporator inlet. The
recuperator is a shell-and-tube heat exchanger (HEX) with plain ﬁns in crossﬂow arrangement [49].
In case of using a recuperator, the enthalpy diﬀerence between the two inlet states of the recuperator
is the possible heat transfer rate and can be found with the following correlation:
˙Qrec = receff · (houtwf,e −houtwf,p)
(10)
where receff is the recuperator eﬃciency, houtwf,e is the enthalpy after the expander and houtwf,p
is the enthalpy after the pump.
Maintaining an energy balance around the recuperator:
houtwf,rec,eva + houtwf,rec,k = houtwf,p + houtwf,e
(11)
where houtwf,rec,eva and houtwf,rec,k are the outlet enthalpies of the recuperator running towards
the evaporator and condenser, respectively.
Considering the energy balance in Eq. (11) the enthalpies of the ﬂuid running towards the evapo-
rator and condenser can be found using the following correlations:
houtwf,rec,eva = houtwf,p + ˙Qrec
(12)
houtwf,rec,k = houtwf,e −˙Qrec
(13)
where Eq. (12) is the enthalpy at the evaporator inlet and Eq. (13) is the enthalpy at the condenser
inlet.
19
==PAGE:36==
2.2
Organic Rankine Cycle
2.2.4
Condenser
The condenser has several purposes, the main one being an isothermal condensation of the remaining
uncondensed vapour to a saturated liquid, in order for the pump to eﬃciently pressurize the liquid
and start over the heat engine cycle.
Moreover, the condenser creates vacuum pressure at the
turbine outlet and allows more vapour to expand [18]. These processes increase the enthalpy drop
after the expander and hence increase the thermal eﬃciency of the cycle, a lower heat sink will
result in a higher enthalpy drop [19]. The heat sink is sea water with an inlet temperature of 20°C
for power generation conﬁgurations and 50°C for cogeneration conﬁgurations.
The total heat rate in the condenser can be calculated with Eq. (14) or (15) depending on the
presence of a recuperator.
˙Qk = ˙mwf · (houtwf,rec,k −hinwf,p)
(14)
˙Qk = ˙mwf · (houtwf,turb −hinwf,p)
(15)
where ˙mwf is the mass ﬂow rate of the working ﬂuid, houtwf,rec,k is the enthalpy at the recuperator
outlet, houtwf,turb is the enthalpy at the turbine outlet and hinwf,p is the enthalpy at the ORC
pump inlet. The mass ﬂow rate of the cold source can then be calculated with Eq. (16):
˙mcs =
˙Qk
Cpwater · Tavg
(16)
where ˙Qk is the heat in the condenser, Cpwater is the speciﬁc heat capacity for water and Tavg is
the average temperature of the cold source and working ﬂuid in the condenser.
The outlet temperature of the cold source is found using Eq. (17).
Tcs,out =
˙Qk
˙mcs · Cpcs,in
(17)
where ˙Qk is the condenser heat, ˙mcs is the mass ﬂow rate of the cold source and Cpcs,in is the heat
capacity of the inlet cold source.
2.2.5
Evaporator
The shell-and-tube HEX is among one of the most common heat exchangers in an ORC because of
its low operation and maintenance cost, and its ability to operate over a broad range of temperatures
and pressures. From a study by Bull et al. [73] it was discovered that plate heat HEXs outperform
20
==PAGE:37==
2.2
Organic Rankine Cycle
shell-and tube HEXs in terms of heat transfer eﬃciency. However, it was also concluded that shell-
and-tube HEXs provide more value to the ORC when incorporating the overall system eﬃciency
and its ability to operate in higher temperatures and pressures. Therefore, the evaporator in this
work consists of a shell-and-tube preheater, evaporator and superheater depending on the cycle
architecture. The mass ﬂow rate of the heat source ( ˙mhs), evaporator outlet temperature of the
heat source (Touths,eva), heat rate from saturated liquid ( ˙QLto,out), latent heat rate ( ˙Qeva,LV ),
preheating heat rate ( ˙Qeva,intoL), temperature of the heat source at saturated liquid (Ths,eva,L)
and temperature of the heat source at saturated vapor (Ths,eva,V ) are computed in diﬀerent ways
depending on conditions and cycle architectures of the ORC. In all conditions ˙Qeva is found using
Eq. (19), but the parameters are computed diﬀerently.
The enthalpy at the evaporator outlet is determined by the enthalpy of the working ﬂuid at saturated
liquid addition to the quality of the ﬂuid:
houtwf,eva = hwf,eva,L + xout,wf,eva · (hwf,eva,V −hwf,eva,L)
(18)
where hwf,eva,L and hwf,eva,V is the enthalpy of the working ﬂuid at saturated liquid and vapour,
respectively, and xout,wf,eva is the quality at the evaporator outlet.
˙Qeva = ˙QLto,out + ˙Qeva,intoL
(19)
where ˙QLto,out is the heat rate from saturated liquid to evaporator outlet and ˙Qeva,intoL is the
preheating heat rate before the saturated liquid phase.

where houtwf,eva is the enthalpy at evaporator outlet and hwf, eva, L is the enthalpy in the
evaporator for saturated liquid.

For SCORCs, a method to increase the thermal eﬃciency is by adding a superheater, which is a
heat exchanger, that has the main purpose of increasing the average temperature at which energy is
added and explore energy of the heat source. Thereby, increasing the overall eﬃciency 1. Naturally,
the maximum turbine inlet temperature is limited by the heat source temperature of the HTF.
However, the addition of a superheater results in a higher component cost.
Depending on the
working ﬂuid’s saturation curve, superheaters may be necessary in case of using a wet ﬂuid [79].
In real life applications, superheaters can also be added to ensure saturated vapor at turbine inlet
to compensate for potential temperature and quality decrease caused by thermal losses. However,
thermal losses in pipes are not considered in this project. The degree of superheat is found using
follow equation:
SHout,eva = Tout,wf,eva −Twf,eva,V
(36)
where Tout,wf,eva is the temperature of the working ﬂuid at evaporator outlet and Twf,eva,V is the
temperature of the working ﬂuid at saturated vapor.

2.2.7
Pumps
The organic Rankine cycle feed pump has the purpose of increasing the pressure after the condenser
to the evaporation pressure so the cycle can repeat itself. The power consumption of this pump
can be computed with the following correlation [35]:
˙Pp = ˙mORC · (Hwf,out,pump,is −Hwf,in,pump)
ηp
(39)
where ˙mORC is the mass ﬂow rate of the organic working ﬂuid, ηp,is is the isentropic eﬃciency of the
feed pump, Hwf,out,pump,is is the isentropic speciﬁc enthalpy at the pump outlet and Hwf,in,pump is
the speciﬁc enthalpy at the pump inlet. However, because the pump motor and inverter eﬃciency
also have to be accounted for, Eq. (39) has to be modiﬁed slightly and can be determined with Eq.
(40) and Eq. (41).
25
==PAGE:42==
2.2
Organic Rankine Cycle
˙Pp = ˙mORC · (Hwf,out,pump,is −Hwf,in,pump)
ηp · ηpm · ηpi
(40)
˙Pp = ˙mORC · (Hwf,out,pump −Hwf,in,pump)
ηpm · ηpi
(41)
where ηpm and ηpi are the ORC pump motor’s and inverter’s eﬃciency, respectively. The speciﬁc
enthalpy at the pump inlet can be found through the refprop library in MATLAB while the speciﬁc
enthalpy at the pump outlet can be determined with:
Hwf,out,pump = Hwf,in,pump + (Hwf,out,pump,is −Hwf,in,pump)
ηp,is
(42)
Furthermore, the speciﬁc rotational speed of the pump is used to determine the pump type and is

Ns,p = 51.6452 · UPM ·

Hp =

The heat transfer ﬂuid (HTF) pump circulates the oil in the solar circuit and the procedure to
determine the pump type is identical to the ORC feed pump. The power consumption of the HTF
pump is however calculated with the pressure drop occurring in the pump instead of using the
enthalpy drop:
˙PSF,p =
˙mSF · ∆P

where ˙mSF is the mass ﬂow rate of the heat transfer ﬂuid, ∆P is the pressure drop in the HTF

ηpm,SF and ηpi,SF is the HTF pump motor and inverter eﬃciency, respectively.
2.3
Solar Collector Field
Concentrated solar power technologies are used to guide light waves from a large area concentrating
to a much smaller area to heat up heat transfer ﬂuids in e.g. pipes. The most common technologies
applied for this purpose are parabolic trough collectors, solar power towers and linear Fresnel
reﬂectors. However, given that all of these collectors use very expensive receivers and heavy glass
mirrors, a new micro-structured polymer foil based collector has been proposed in a recent study
[15]. This type of collector can be applied in systems with temperatures up to 350°C and has a
low installation and O&M cost compared to other solar collectors on the market. In addition to
being a renewable, concentrated solar collectors are cost competitive to power produced by fossil
fuels [13].
The heat transfer rate in the micro-structured polymer foil-based concentrating solar collector
system, can be calculated with the following correlations [25][26][27]:
˙QSF = ηo,SF ·DNI·IAM ·ηend,SF ·ηshadow,SF ·AP,SF −Ul,SF ·(Tm,SF −Ta)·AP,SF −˙Qpipe·AP,SF (46)
˙QSF = ˙mSF · Cp,avg · (Tout,SF −Tin,SF )
(47)
where ηo,SF is the optical eﬃency of the solar ﬁeld, DNI is the direct normal irradiance and IAM
is the incidence angle modiﬁer which is equal to 1 for the CSP system because the collector axis is
oriented as two-axis tracking. The shadow-losses (ηshadow,SF ), end-losses (ηend,SF ) and heat losses
in pipes ( ˙Qpipe) are usually calculated during the detailed engineering design stage [15] but are
neglected in this work. The shadow and end losses are typically around 3% [28]. The solar collector
ﬁeld’s ﬁrst order heat-loss coeﬃcient (Ul,SF ) is based on the aperture area of the solar collector
(AP,SF ). The temperatures Tm,SF and Ta are the mean temperatures of the solar collector ﬁeld
and the ambient temperature, respectively. The solar collector eﬃciency is determined with [15]:
ηSF = ηo,SF −Ul,SF · (Tm,SF −Ta)
DNI
−U2,SF · (Tm,SF −Ta)2
DNI
(48)
where the second order heat loss coeﬃcient U2,SF is assumed to be zero because the heat loss from
the pipes is neglected.
27
==PAGE:44==
2.3
Solar Collector Field
The required area of the solar ﬁeld can then be found, and is deﬁned by Eq. (49):
AP,SF =
˙QSF
ηSF · DNI
(49)
where ˙QSF is the heat rate of the solar ﬁeld, ηSF is the eﬃciency of the solar ﬁeld and DNI is the
direct normal irradiance.
Solar ﬁelds are usually oversized because solar plants are dynamic and it is therefore necessary to
evaluate the magnitude of this over-sizing. The solar multiple (SM) is deﬁned as the ratio between
heat produced by the solar ﬁeld at design condition and heat required by the power block at nominal
conditions. The SM greatly aﬀects the area and cost of the system and is based on location, solar
ﬁeld type and storage capacity.
However, the same value of SM will be used when comparing
ORC conﬁgurations, and will, relatively, not aﬀect the design condition results signiﬁcantly. SM is
fundamentally a size parameter that designs the solar area for when the DNI is lower than at design
condition, by oversizing it. Typically, the optimum value of solar multiple is determined for each
conﬁguration for a given place and a given thermal energy storage capacity based on the annual
simulations. In the present analysis, for simpliﬁcation, a ﬁxed value of solar multiple is taken as
2,2 which is suitable for places like Antofagasta, Chile, with very high annual DNI. The actual area
is then deﬁned in Eq. (50):
AP,SF,actual = AP,SF · SM
(50)
2.3.1
Heat Transfer Fluids
The Eastman Therminol 55 heat transfer ﬂuid is designed for low pressure heating systems and has
a consistent heat transfer performance during its lifetime at maximum bulk temperatures up to 290
°C [12]. It has shown to be very advantageous compared to other mineral oil-based heat transfer
ﬂuids due to its cost-eﬀectiveness and chemical properties. Furthermore, it is highly resistant to
fouling compared to mineral oils because of its synthetic nature. The thermodynamic properties
associated with Therminol 55 are taken from a data catalogue provided by Eastman chemical
company [12].
The cost of rapeseed oil is more than 5 times lower than the cost of Therminol 55, hence making it
a cheap alternative to mineral oils and other synthetic heat transfer ﬂuids. The use of vegetable oils
is also a promising solution in terms of sustainable development, given that mineral or synthetic oils
are petreloum-based which is a depleting resource in contrast to vegetable oils that have very high
availability. It was also discovered in a study from J.-F. Hoﬀmann et al. that the thermophysical
properties of vegetable oils are on average 13% superior to the Therminol VP1 ones and the biggest
disadvantage of rapeseed oil is its limited maximal operating temperature at around 250°C [57].
Therefore, considering safety margins the maximum temperature of rapeseed oil should not exceed
28
==PAGE:45==

Thermal Energy Storage
220°C. From J.-F. Hoﬀmann et al. the density and speciﬁc heat capacity of vegetable oils can be
determined with extrapolation by Eq. (51) and (52):
ρ(T) = a · T + b
(51)
Cp(T) = f · T 4 + g · T 3 + h · T 2 + i · T + j
(52)
where the coeﬃcients are derived from the study performed by Hoﬀman et al.
and T is the
temperature. The density and speciﬁc heat capacity for rapeseed oil can be calculated speciﬁcally
with Eq. (53) and (54):

(54)
where T is the temperature.
2.4
Thermal Energy Storage
Three types of thermal energy storage are investigated and evaluated. The ﬁrst storage application,
two-tank indirect storage, consists of a cold and hot water tank connected to the solar ﬁeld through
a heat exchanger, where the content of the two tanks is molten salt. The second storage application,
two-tank direct storage, also consists of a cold and hot water tank but instead of storing the heat
indirectly with a heat exchanger the heat transfer ﬂuid from the solar collector ﬁeld is directly
stored in the tanks. The third storage application, packed-bed rock storage, consists of one tank
packed with small rocks, that are heated or cooled directly from the heat transfer ﬂuid. The thermal
energy storage systems are previously illustrated in Figures 1, 2 and 3.
The thermal energy storage capacity (Qst) for all conﬁgurations is calculated with Eq. (55) [15][16]:
Qst = ˙Qin · t
(55)
where ˙Qin is the thermal power input at design condition, t is the equivalent hours of storage. The
thermal losses from the storage are neglected.

==PAGE:46==

Thermal Energy Storage

design condition which can be calculated with the following correlations [15]:

2.5
Cogeneration
2.5
Cogeneration
Cogeneration is per deﬁnition when two diﬀerent forms of energy are generated from a single energy
source, in this case the two diﬀerent forms of energy are mechanical and thermal. The mechanical
energy is used for electricity production and the by-product, thermal energy, is used for distillation.
Cogeneration can be used to signiﬁcantly improve energy eﬃciency and revenue by the usage
the cycle’s by-product [74][75].
With implementation of cogeneration the additional cost of a
second energy application and miscellaneous must be considered. Furthermore, most cogeneration
technologies use a higher than ambient temperature heat sink, and require a higher condensation
temperature and pressure. Normally, with higher condensation temperature and pressure, a lower
enthalpy drop occurs in the turbine. Hence, less mechanical power will be produced by the turbine,
thereby decreasing the thermal eﬃciency, when considering only power generation. However, solar
plants are usually designed on a ﬁxed power output. Therefore, Pgross = 1 MW is assumed and
the mechanical power in the turbine will naturally not be reduced. The required heat input of
the system will increase instead. Consequently, decreasing the thermal eﬃciency of the system
and increasing the cost. For cogeneration plants, the heat that would otherwise be rejected to
the heat sink is recovered to provide an additional useful eﬀect, generating revenues. In order to
assess the feasibility of a cogeneration system, it is crucial to perform an economic analysis of the
whole system. This analysis will determine whether the additional revenues from the by-product
compensate the increase in system cost and eventually lead to higher overall revenues of the system.
2.5.1
Desalination of Seawater
Desalination of seawater can be done by separating saline from seawater through multi eﬀect distil-
lation. Saline water is heated and vaporized, using waste heat from the ORC’s condenser, through
consecutive eﬀects, where freshwater and brine are collected at each stage. At each stage more
sea water is condensed and more brine is extracted, resulting in salt-free water. The concentrated
remaining saltwater is then let back into the sea. In order to make the distilled water into domestic
water it has its PH-value adjusted, minerals added and harmful bacteria killed. MED is vastly
used in countries in the Gulf Cooperation Council [77], as their saltwater accessibility makes it an
ideal solution to their dry climate. It is suitable for large-scale applications due to its superior heat
transfer characteristics, low LCOW [78] and low pump electricity consumption in comparison to
other desalination processes [77].
The location of the case study, Antofagasta, a port city in the northern part of Chile, has had fresh
water scarcity for years [30]. According to a study by M. Molinos-Senante and G. Donoso [31] the
southern region of Chile has a much higher water availability than the northern regions of Chile,
which consequently caused the freshwater prices to be higher in the northern part of the country
[31]. Cogeneration, using waste thermal energy, to generate freshwater through desalination of
34
==PAGE:51==
2.6
System Performance
sea water is investigated, in an attempt to reduce water scarcity and reduce carbonization, by
increasing energy eﬃciency. The cold source inlet and outlet temperature are changed to 50 °C
and 80 °C, respectively.
The energy consumption for heat and pump are taken from a multi-eﬀect-plate-evaporator made
to distillate seawater to produce freshwater on board of large ships [32]. The ﬁxed freshwater price
in the Antofagasta region in 2016 was 1,5 euro/m3 [33]. The input parameters are given in Table 6

2.6
System Performance
The performance of the system can be assessed in several ways. When comparing diﬀerent system
conﬁgurations to one another, the capital investment cost, LCOE and LCOW are the primary
parameters optimized in order to ﬁnd the cheapest solutions. However, various other system per-
formance parameters, i.e. system eﬃciencies, should also be considered to further evaluate and
analyse the results. The correlation for thermal eﬃciency (ηth) for power only generation ORCs is
the ratio between electricity output and heat input and is presented in Eq.(79) [44]. The thermal
35
==PAGE:52==
2.7
Cost Correlations
eﬃciency is not considered when cogeneration is active, as thermal losses are not included and thus
the eﬃciency will be near 100%.
ηth =
˙Pnet
QH
(79)
where ˙Pnet is the net power output of the system and QH is the thermal heat for the working ﬂuid
in the evaporator.
For applications of medium-scale, such as considered in this evaluation, the thermal eﬃciency for
PEORCs is typically lower than for SCORCs.
PEORCs cannot use high eﬃciency turbine ex-
panders. Instead PEORCs have to use screw expanders that can operate in the two-phase state
as a wet expansion, but are not designed for applications of such scale [46], hence reducing the
isentropic expander eﬃciency [47]. Wet expansions do however typically have a higher exergy eﬃ-
ciency [41], due to better matching of heat source and working ﬂuid temperature proﬁles [48], which
thermodynamically leads to a better heat transfer and reduction of entropy generation equating to
smaller heat losses according to the 2nd law of thermodynamics [50]. Exergy (second law) eﬃciency
for power production can be used to measure the energy ratio that can be utilized from the system
and can be deﬁned as the ratio between exergy recovered and exergy supplied:
ηII =
˙Pgross
˙mhs · ehs,in
(80)
where ˙Pgross is the gross electricity output (exergy recovered), ˙mhs is the mass ﬂow rate of the heat
source and is, multiplied with ehs,in, which is the speciﬁc exergy diﬀerence between the heat source
and the ambient conditions, the exergy supplied. The exergy diﬀerence between the two conditions
can be found using the following correlation:
ehs,in = cpmhs · (Tinhs −Ta) −Ta · (shs,in −shs,a)
(81)
where cpmhs is the speciﬁc heat capacity of the heat source, Tinhs is the heat source temperature
at evaporator inlet, Ta is the ambient temperature and (shs,in −shs,a) is the entropy diﬀerence
between the heat source at evaporator inlet and at ambient temperature.

2.8
Economic Model
2.8
Economic Model
The total investment cost consists of ﬁve separate costs for power only generation, Eq. (120), and
six separate costs for cogeneration, Eq. (121). The total investment cost is the sum of all considered
systems: power block (CPB), thermal energy storage (CTES), solar ﬁeld (CSF ), civil work Ccivil,
miscellaneous Cmisc and MED-system (CMED) for cogeneration.
Csys,power = CPB + CTES + CSF + Ccivil + Cmisc
(120)
Csys,cogen = CPB + CTES + CSF + Ccivil + Cmisc + CMED
(121)
Ccivil and Cmisc are constant for all cycles, as they are entirely dependent on Pgross.
CPB is the total cost of all components in the power block, included in the previous section, Cost
Correlations. In order to account for contingency and fee costs, the cost is multiplied with 15%
and 3%, respectively, as suggested by R. Turton et al. [80]. Furthermore, the cost correlations
presented are in US$ and therefore need to be corrected to € with a correction factor of 1,2.
CPB = Ccomp · 1, 18
1, 2
(122)
where Ccomp is the sum of the components in the power block and 1,18 and 1,2 are the correction
factors.
CTES is the total cost of the thermal energy storage:
CTES = CST + Ctank + Cbalance
(123)
where CST is the cost of the storage medium, Ctank is the cost of the tank and Cbalance is the cost
of balancing the two storages, including consumption of two pumps.
CSF is the total cost of the solar ﬁeld, as presented in Eq. (112).
CMED is the total cost of the multi-eﬀect distillation system, as presented in Eq. (119).
In order to evaluate the proﬁtability of a project the net present value (NPV) of the project has to
be accounted for and analysed. The net present value can be calculated with Eq. (124):
NPV = Csys + COM −
C1
(1 + r)1 −
C2
(1 + r)2 −... −
Cn
(1 + r)n
(124)
where Csys is the initial investment cost and COM is the total annualized O&M cost throughout
the lifetime of the system, n is the number of periods, C1, C2 & Cn are cash ﬂows for each period
and r is the discount rate of the project. The cash ﬂows are respectively the yearly electricity net
43
==PAGE:60==
2.8
Economic Model
output multiplied with the electricity price and, during cogeneration, also the yearly freshwater
volume multiplied with the freshwater price.
The discount rate and lifetime of the project are assumed to be 3% and 30 years, respectively. If
NPV>0 then the discounted present value of future cash ﬂows is positive, hence making the project
feasible.
By that deﬁnition, the PBT is the number of years the project takes to have NPV = 0.
The levelized cost of electricity for the only power generating system can be calculated with Eq.
(125) [15].
LCOE = (Csys + COM)
Epower
(125)
where Csys is the initial investment cost, CO&M and Epower are the annualized O&M cost and total
electricity revenue throughout the plant’s lifetime, respectively.
During cogeneration mode the condensation pressure increases, which consequently reduces the
thermal eﬃciency. In addition to the MED cost, the system also needs a larger heat input, and
therefore larger investment cost, but can now generate a revenue from selling freshwater.
The
levelized cost of freshwater generation for the cogeneration plant can be calculated with Eq. (126)
[15].
LCOW = (Csys,cogen + COM) −(LCOE · Ecogen)
VFW
(126)
where Csys,cogen is the initial investment cost of the ORC cogeneration plant, CO&M is the total
annualized operation and maintenance cost throughout the plant’s lifetime, VFW is the total annu-
alized volume of the freshwater production throughout the plant’s lifetime, LCOE is the levelized
cost of electricity for power only generation and Ecogen is the total annualized electricity production
for the cogeneration plant throughout the plant’s lifetime.
It can be diﬃcult to compare only power generation conﬁguration plants with cogeneration conﬁg-
uration plants using LCOE and LCOW, hence the pay-back time (PBT) for a project is incredibly
useful, as it considers revenues and costs from both conﬁguration plants.
A study by S. Yang et al. [95] concluded that monetary incentive for renewables positively aﬀected
the newly installed renewable capacity in developing countries. But according to IRENA [96] no
ﬁscal incentive for renewables is yet present in Chile. A monetary incentive on renewables can
improve the advantage of the conﬁgurations in the present study.
44
==PAGE:61==
3
Results
This section contains the results of the thesis. The goal of the thesis is to perform a techno-economic
evaluation of partial evaporation cycles.
Therefore, the majority of results are investigated in
relation to capital investment cost, LCOE, LCOW and PBT. First, the power only generation cycles
are optimized and the best performing conﬁgurations are presented. Next, cogeneration cycles are
optimized and the best performing conﬁgurations are presented and ultimately compared with one
another.
3.1
Model Validation
In order to validate the model’s results it is necessary to compare to similar studies. It is highly
unusual for a PEORC to operate at medium-temperatures for solar thermal panels, hence minus-
cule data is available regarding the performance of such cycles. The initial MATLAB model was
developed for SCORC and PEORC for low temperature geothermal applications maximizing power
output, done by Pilli et al. [49] and modiﬁed by C. Tammone. The cost correlations are used from
Astolﬁ’s Ph.d. thesis [51], and are based on real components’ cost from the manufacturer.
Furthermore, results from similar studies for low-medium scale solar integrated ORCs are compared
to the results found in this study. In a study by N. Desai et al. [15] for a 1 MW net electricity
output solar integrated SCORC with a MED system located in Chile the LCOE is 0,141 €/kWh
and LCOW is 1,42 €/m3. The LCOW is expected to be higher in this thesis, as less freshwater
is produced. Furthermore, in reference [15] the area of solar collectors is calculated to be around
18.000 m2 at a LCOW = 1, 4. A variation in results is expected, as annual simulations have been
run for the reference study [15], consequently using a diﬀerent capacity factor. Moreover, diﬀerent
cost correlations have been used for the power cycle, which can give variations in LCOE and LCOW
as well. The economic model for the SCORC is validated in Table 7.
Table 7: Validation of economical model.

3.2
Power Generation
This section contains results from power only generation ORCs. The thermodynamic states and
conditions for each storage technology for rapeseed oil and Therminol 55 can be seen in the summary
tables presented in the upcoming sections using the working ﬂuid that resulted in the lowest capital
investment cost.
3.2.1
Working Fluids
An investigation on the diﬀerent working ﬂuids’ quality inﬂuence on the capital investment cost pr.
net output is conducted. The best performing working ﬂuids are used for designing and comparing
each storage technology. For both rapeseed oil and Therminol 55 the trends are similar for all
working ﬂuids and storage technologies: the speciﬁc investment cost decreases the closer the ﬂuid
46
==PAGE:63==
3.2
Power Generation
approaches saturated vapor state.
Figure 10, 11 and 12 show the relation between speciﬁc investment cost and ﬂuid quality using
Therminol 55 for packed-bedrock TES, 2T-direct TES and 2T-indirect TES, respectively.
Figure 10: Working ﬂuids for rock with Therminol
55.
Figure 11: Working ﬂuids for direct with Therminol
55.
The cheapest working ﬂuid for the packed-bedrock TES using Therminol 55 is cyclopentane for
TLORC and toluene for PEORC. The choice of working ﬂuid is less signiﬁcant than for rapeseed
oil, as the most expensive working ﬂuid is only around 20% more expensive than cyclopentane at
saturated liquid. This is due to smaller expander pressure diﬀerences between the working ﬂuids.
The curves are continuously decreasing after x = 0.1, whereas the rapeseed oil curves are more ﬂat
after x = 0.1. The cheapest working ﬂuid for the 2T-direct TES using Therminol 55 is cyclopentane
for TLORC and PEORC at saturated vapor state.
The 2T-indirect TES is the least favourable storage technology for low qualities, seen on Figure 12,
as molten salt’s minimum temperature of 165 °C limits the advantage of having a lower quality ﬂuid
[41], thereby limiting the temperature span of the heat source. A low temperature diﬀerence of
the heat source increases the storage cost signiﬁcantly. A lower quality ﬂuid is more advantageous
for storage technologies that permit a high heat source temperature diﬀerence, hence 2T-direct
TES with Therminol 55 having a slightly less steep working ﬂuid curve than packed-bedrock TES
with Therminol 55 and much lower costs. It therefore appears more advantageous for systems using
Therminol 55 to have a high ﬂuid quality than for systems using rapeseed oil. The cheapest working
ﬂuid for 2T-indirect TES using Therminol 55 is toluene for TLORC and PEORC. For SCORCs
using Therminol 55, cyclopentane is the best performing ﬂuid.
47
==PAGE:64==
3.2
Power Generation
Figure 12: Diﬀerent working ﬂuids’ quality inﬂuence on capital investment cost for 2T-indirect
thermal energy storage with Therminol 55.
The volume ratio is the density ratio between turbine outlet and inlet, where for TLORCs the
turbine inlet ﬂuid is saturated liquid, which, for some working ﬂuids, has a density considerably
higher than its saturated liquid-vapor mixture, thus causing large volume ratios. A study by B.
Saadatfar et al. [36] reported that high critical temperature working ﬂuids with low condensing
pressures result in large volume ratios and thus larger and more costly turbines.
However, K.
Darvish et al. [83] concluded that one of the most key parameters for a working ﬂuid’s performance
was its pressure ratio before and after the expansion, i.e. pressure ratio between evaporation and
condensation pressure. However, two working ﬂuids operating under the same pressure ratio do not
necessarily have the same performance. The enthalpy drop in the expander also depends on the
molecular complexity and position on the T-s diagram. Furthermore, heavy molecules cause poor
heat transfer coeﬃcients [58] and thus a larger heat input is required, consequently increasing the
cost. Working ﬂuid conductivity inﬂuences the heat transfer and the temperature proﬁles in the
evaporator and therefore greatly impacts the irreversibility and energy utilization. When a working
ﬂuids reaches its maximum allowed pressure ratio it performs well.
Pressure ratios in the expander for the investigated working ﬂuids are listed in Table 9 for TLORC
with packed-bedrock TES using rapeseed oil and Therminol 55, respectively. As showed in Table 9,
some of the working ﬂuids perform diﬀerently for rapeseed oil at 220 °C and Therminol 55 at 278
°C. The pressure diﬀerence is permitted to be higher by having a higher heat source temperature
[84] hence toluene’s and chlorobenzene’s improved performance for systems using Therminol 55 as
HTF. Cyclopentane performs well for both rapeseed oil and Therminol 55, as both heat source and
condensing temperatures are respectively high and low enough to increase the pressure diﬀerence
considerably.
48
==PAGE:65==
3.2
Power Generation

In real life applications low condensing pressures are exposed to having inﬁltration of non-condensable
gases from the environment, and can therefore increase complexity and cost of the system.
Figure 13 and Figure 14 show the relation between speciﬁc investment cost and ﬂuid quality using
rapeseed oil for packed-bedrock TES and 2T-direct TES, respectively.
Figure 13: Working ﬂuids for bedrock with rapeseed oil. Figure 14: Working ﬂuids for direct with rapeseed oil.
Cyclopentane is the cheapest working ﬂuid for TLORC and PEORC at saturated vapor for packed-
bedrock TES using rapeseed oil. The choice of working ﬂuid appears to be very important, especially
for TLORC architectures, as the speciﬁc investment costs for MDM, ethylbenzene and MM are
nearly 50% higher than cyclopentane’s, due to their density change from saturated liquid. It is
also clear, that cyclopentane’s and hexane’s speciﬁc costs do not increase signiﬁcantly with low
ﬂuid quality. For rapeseed oil the two investigated storage technologies have very similar trends,
with packed-bedrock TES being slightly cheaper. The reason to their similarity is due to their heat
49
==PAGE:66==
3.2
Power Generation
source outlet temperatures, which are nearly the same. The TES cost increases signiﬁcantly when
the heat source temperature diﬀerence (∆Ths) decreases, because a much larger mass of storage ﬂuid
is required for a small ∆Ths, in order to meet the same heat input requirement to the ORC circuit.
Furthermore, the same heat transfer ﬂuid at the same heat source temperature is used, therefore
similarity is expected for the two thermal storages. Cyclopentane is also the cheapest working
ﬂuid for the TLORC and PEORC at saturated vapor for the 2T-direct TES using rapeseed oil.
Ultimatively cyclopentane is the best performing working ﬂuid for all storage technologies when
using rapeseed oil as HTF.

3.2

In Figure 15 and 16 the T-s diagrams for the TLORC and SCORC architecture are shown. The
TLORC has a lower inlet temperature of the solar ﬁeld which is expected because it can exchange
heat with the heat source at lower temperatures. Additionally, the TLORC’s exergy eﬃciency for
power production is higher compared to the SCORC because it can extract a larger amount of heat
from the heat source [48]. However, the exergetic eﬃciency diﬀerence is only 5% for the TLORC
and SCORC, which may be caused by the high inlet temperature of the heat source. The TLORC
also has a higher mass ﬂow rate of the working ﬂuid compared to the SCORC due to its higher
heat input. The thermal eﬃciency of the TLORC will hence be lower compared to the SCORC
because the power output remains the same [6] and since the thermal storage cost also increases
for the TLORC this cycle architecture is proven to be disadvantageous compared to the SCORC.
Moreover, it is seen that the isentropic eﬃciency of the TLORC is lower than the SCORC’s, even
though the same turbine eﬃciency correlations have been used for both cycles. This is caused by
the wet expansion occurring in the TLORC [82].
Figure 15: T-s diagram for TLORC.
Figure 16: T-s diagram for SCORC.
Furthermore, the choice of the working ﬂuid for the TLORC is rather unusual because the tem-
perature proﬁles are not parallelly connected to each other. A working ﬂuid with higher critical
temperature was expected, because a higher turbine inlet temperature usually leads to a higher
thermal eﬃciency. The bad heat exchange curve in the TLORC is caused by the low critical tem-
perature of cyclopentane, which can be seen in Table 4, and the high inlet temperature of the
heat source. However, from the working ﬂuid investigation it was shown that ﬂuids with higher
critical temperatures than cyclopentane, such as toluene, had a higher speciﬁc capital investment
cost compared to cyclopentane.
Therefore, cyclopentane was selected as working ﬂuid for the
TLORC even though the temperature proﬁles are uncharacteristic. The heat input is increasing
signiﬁcantly if the working ﬂuid has a higher critical temperature, hence making cyclopentane a
52
==PAGE:69==
3.2
Power Generation
better option. This discovery is a clear indication of the SCORC being superior to the TLORC,
given that a TLORC with toluene as working ﬂuid and parallel temperature proﬁles performs worse
than a TLORC with cyclopentane as working ﬂuid and non-parallel temperature proﬁles. It also
indicates that the heat exchanger cost is not a signiﬁcant share of the total capital investment cost,
since cyclopentane with a poor heat exchange curve in the evaporator is cheaper than toluene with
matching temperature proﬁles. The optimum pinch point temperature for the SCORC is also lower
compared to the TLORC, which shows that the heat curve for the SCORC is a better match for
this conﬁguration. The beneﬁt of a TLORC is its capability of extracting a large amount of heat
from a heat source by having parallel temperature proﬁles in the evaporator close to each other.
However, the heat exchange curve for the TLORC has a high evaporator pinch point temperature
on the cold side of the heat exchanger and the temperature proﬁles do not match as in a traditional
TLORC, proving that the TLORC design is not suitable for this conﬁguration.
53
==PAGE:70==

Power Generation

The T-s diagrams for the TLORC and SCORC are illustrated in Figure 17 and 18. Many of the

==PAGE:73==
3.2
Power Generation
The T-s diagrams for the TLORC and SCORC are displayed in Figure 19 and 20. The working
ﬂuid in the TLORC is toluene while the working ﬂuid in the SCORC is cyclopentane. The inlet
temperature of the solar ﬁeld is restricted to 165°C, making it a necessity for the TLORC to
have its corresponding evaporator pinch point temperature on the hot side of the heat exchanger.
Furthermore, the inlet temperature of the solar ﬁeld remains the same for TLORC and SCORC
due to this limitation, and the possible beneﬁts of achieving a lower outlet temperature of the heat
source in the evaporator is thus removed for the TLORC. A noticeable value is the condensation
temperature for the TLORC, which is 75 °C. If the condensation temperature is lower, then the
temperature diﬀerence between the heat source outlet and working ﬂuid inlet is extremely high,
leading to a very poor heat transfer. With the use of a recuperator the condensation temperature
is reduced and the evaporator inlet working ﬂuid temperature is increased, thus reducing the
temperature diﬀerence between the heat source outlet and working ﬂuid inlet.
Therefore, the TLORC cycle architecture is performing considerably worse for the indirect-storage
compared to the direct and thermocline storage.
Figure 19: T-s diagram for TLORC.
Figure 20: T-s diagram for SCORC.
Comparison and Feasibility
The speciﬁc capital cost of TLORC, PEORC and SCORC with packed-bed rock, direct and indirect
TES using Therminol 55 are shown in Figure 21. The solar ﬁeld cost contributes to around half
of the speciﬁc cost for all of the conﬁgurations and cycles, making this part of the power plant
vital in terms of optimizing the entire plant. PEORCs usually have a lower thermal eﬃciency than
SCORCs thus requiring a higher heat input for the same power output. The heat input for the
PEORC is consequently increasing a lot when the quality of the working ﬂuid in the evaporator is
reduced hence also increasing the cost of the solar ﬁeld. The PEORC will as a result of this try to
57
==PAGE:74==
3.2
Power Generation
achieve the highest quality possible to minimize the solar ﬁeld cost and the optimum quality for the
PEORC is thus 100%. Therefore, the SCORC outperforms the PEORC for all the conﬁgurations
using Therminol 55 as HTF since the change in TES and powerblock cost is insigniﬁcant compared
to the solar ﬁeld cost.
Figure 21: Cost comparison for the three diﬀerent storage conﬁgurations and three diﬀerent cycles
using Therminol 55 as heat transfer ﬂuid.
The feasibility of the TLORC using Therminol 55 as HTF for the direct and packed-bedrock storage
is determined from selection diagrams depicted in Figure 22 and 23.
The indirect storage is never feasible for the TLORC and a selection diagram for this TES is
therefore not shown. The cost of the power block for the TLORC is always higher than the power
block for the SCORC [2], making it necessary for the equal speciﬁc capital cost line to intersect
with the equal speciﬁc cost line of the power block in order for the TLORC to be competitive with
the SCORC. This intersection is not taking place in any of the selection diagrams and the TLORC
for direct and packed-bed rock storage with Therminol 55 is therefore not competitive with the
SCORC. The actual ratio between the design isentropic eﬃciency for TLORC and SCORC is 0,93
in both diagrams and the TLORC is therefore not even competitive with the SCORC if the cost
of the power block was 0. For both TES types to be feasible the cost of the power block for the
TLORC has to be at least 3 times lower than the SCORC power block cost, while also having
the same isentropic eﬃciency in the turbine. This may be very hard to accomplish in real life
applications given that the power block cost of the TLORC is always higher than the power block
cost of the SCORC.
58
==PAGE:75==
3.2
Power Generation
Figure 22: Selection diagram for 2T-direct TES with Therminol 55.
The mass ﬂow rate of the working ﬂuid for the TLORC is also higher compared to the SCORC
as both cycles have to produce 1 MW electricity and the enthalpy drop from the evaporator to
the turbine outlet is smaller for the TLORC, which can be derived from Eq. (2). This results in
a larger power consumption for the pumps in the TLORC [91] and larger heat exchangers, which
increases the cost and reduces the net power output. The isentropic eﬃciency of the turbine is
also heavily aﬀected by the wet expansion that occours in the evaporator in TLORCs because the
isentropic eﬃciency reduces with the moisture content [82].

==PAGE:78==
3.2
Power Generation
The T-s diagrams for TLORC and SCORC can be seen in Figure 24 and Figure 25. Cyclopentane
is used for all conﬁgurations for rapeseed oil, as it has the lowest speciﬁc investment cost. The
PEORC has a vapour quality of 100% at the turbine inlet, thereby behaving like a SCORC. The
inlet temperature of the solar ﬁeld is signiﬁcantly lower for the TLORC, which decreases the capital
cost of the thermal energy storage. However, the cost of the thermal storage is also dependent on the
heat input to the ORC power system, which is considerably higher for the TLORCs, as suggested
by literature [76]. A higher heat input naturally also leads to a higher cost of solar ﬁeld and land,
which is concluded to be a signiﬁcant expense. The work input to the ORC feed pump is much
higher for TLORC [91], because the working ﬂuid is pumped to a high-pressure saturated liquid,
where a higher evaporation pressure is required. Additionally, it is also because the mass ﬂow
rate of the working ﬂuid is higher for the TLORC, due to a higher heat input is required. The
temperature diﬀerence of the heat source is much larger, and thus requires a larger working ﬂuid
mass ﬂow rate, which is known to increase heat absorption and power output [91]. The pinch
temperature diﬀerence for TLORCs is higher than for SCORCs, since a low pinch for TLORCs
results in very large heat exchangers [97], as the heat source and working ﬂuid temperature proﬁles
are nearly parallel.
Figure 24: T-s diagram for TLORC.
Figure 25: T-s diagram for SCORC.

The T-s diagrams for TLORC and SCORC are seen in Figure 26 and 27, respectively. The vapour
quality at turbine inlet is also 100% for PEORC, hence behaving like a SCORC. There are identical
trends occurring for packed-bedrock and 2T-direct TES, with lower mass ﬂow rate of the heat source
and inlet temperature of the solar ﬁeld and thus also higher temperature gain in the evaporator and
mass ﬂow rate of the working ﬂuid. The working ﬂuid turbine inlet temperature for the SCORC
is high, because of the 48 degrees superheat added in the evaporator. The PEORC behaves like
a SCORC, but is limited by the optimization code, and does not have the opportunity to have a
superheater. Instead, the working ﬂuid evaporator inlet temperature is lower than the SCORC’s
and the mass ﬂow rate of the working ﬂuid is slightly higher. A noticeable amount of heat is left
in the condenser for the TLORC, why cogeneration may be advantageous.

==PAGE:81==
3.2
