Comparison and Feasibility
A cost comparison of the two investigated thermal energy storage technologies using rapeseed oil
as HTF with three diﬀerent cycle architectures can be seen in Figure 28. Many of the same trends
occurring when using Therminol 55 also occur when using rapeseed oil. The solar ﬁeld cost has the
largest impact on the speciﬁc capital cost and the quality of the working ﬂuid in the evaporator
for the PEORC is also 100% when optimized freely. However, the storage cost of the TLORC
using rapeseed oil as HTF with direct storage is only 16% of the total capital cost whereas it is
25% for the TLORC with direct storage using Therminol 55. This is due to the price diﬀerence
between rapeseed oil and Therminol 55, hence making the storage cost smaller for the direct TES
using rapeseed oil as HTF. It is clear that, relatively, the TLORC’s storage cost is lower than the
SCORC’s storage cost, due to a larger required mass of the storage ﬂuid caused by a lower heat
source temperature diﬀerence. Studies [91] [92] suggest that the trilateral cycle performs better
at lower temperatures, because the additional heat recovered is higher with respect to the total
heat input when compared to higher temperature heat sources. Additionally, the SCORC performs
worse than the TLORC at lower temperatures, as a lower turbine inlet temperature results in a
lower thermal eﬃciency [90]. This relatively increases the performance of the TLORC, however not
enough to compete with the SCORC.
Figure 28: Cost comparison for the two diﬀerent storage conﬁgurations and three diﬀerent cycles
using rapeseed oil as heat transfer ﬂuid.
The feasiblity of the TLORCs using rapeseed oil as HTF for the direct and packed-bed rock TES
is determined from diagrams shown in Figure 29 and 30. As beforementioned, the indirect storage
65
==PAGE:82==
3.2
Power Generation
with rapeseed oil as HTF is not investigated.
Figure 29: Selection diagram for direct TES with rapeseed oil.
Figure 30: Selection diagram for packed-bedrock TES with rapeseed oil.
The intersection between the equal speciﬁc capital cost line for both systems and the equal speciﬁc
66
==PAGE:83==
3.2
Power Generation
cost of the power block for both systems is not taking place in any of the selection diagrams and
the TLORCs for both TES types with rapeseed oil as HTF are therefore deemed infeasible to
compete with the SCORC. In comparison to using Therminol 55 as HTF, the competitiveness to
the SCORC has increased considerably for both storage technologies, as to be expected from Figure
28, where it is seen that the costs for the TLORCs have decreased and the costs for the SCORCs
have increased, thus relatively a signiﬁcant change.
The ratios between the design isentropic eﬃciencies for TLORC and SCORC are 0,92 and 0,90 for
2T-direct and packed-bedrock TES, respectively. In order for the TLORCs to be feasible at these
isentropic eﬃciencies the cost of the TLORC’s power block must be 65% and 50% of the SCORC’s
power block for direct and bedrock storage, respectively.
3.2.4
Comparison
Figure 31 shows a cost comparison for the storage technologies using Therminol 55 and rapeseed
oil, respectively. Rapeseed oil is advantageous for 2T-direct TES and the TLORCs. The direct TES
uses only rapeseed oil as storage ﬂuid, whereas packed-bedrock TES also uses rocks. Therefore a
signiﬁcant decrease in storage ﬂuid price also has a signiﬁcant decrease in storage cost. Naturally,
having a cheaper storage cost is also advantageous for SCORCs, however it does not compensate
for decreasing the heat source temperature that is necessary when using rapeseed oil.
Figure 31: Cost comparison of storage technologies using Therminol 55 and rapeseed oil.
Higher heat source temperatures lead to higher turbine inlet temperatures and studies [89] [90]
report a higher thermal eﬃciency when turbine inlet temperature is increased. This is especially
67
==PAGE:84==
3.2
Power Generation
applicable for SCORC architectures, as the implementation of a superheater can help exploit high
heat source temperatures, but also increase the desuperheating in the condenser. Despite rapeseed
oil is a ﬁfth of the price of Therminol 55, the rapeseed oil conﬁgurations are still not cheaper for
SCORCs. However, for TLORCs rapeseed oil is advantageous due to its lower heat source inlet
temperature and cheaper storage cost. The cyclopentane based SCORC using packed-bedrock TES
with Therminol is the conﬁguration with the lowest speciﬁc investment cost, with PEORC using
Therminol being the second lowest, as it is behaving like a SCORC. The TLORC is concluded to
never be competitive with the SCORC for these conditions.
68
==PAGE:85==
3.3
Cogeneration
3.3
Cogeneration
In this section the results from the cogeneration ORCs are presented. The result summary tables
and T-s diagrams for each case are presented in Appendices A.1 to A.5. They are not shown in
the result section because the same trends are present as in the power generation only summary
tables. However, the condensation temperature is increased to 80-85 °C, hence increasing the size
of the systems.
3.3.1
Working Fluids
The working ﬂuid with the best performance for power only generation does not necessarily have
the best performance when cogenerating, as cogeneration has a diﬀerent thermodynamic cycle.
Therefore, it is necessary to investigate each ﬂuid’s cogeneration performance.
The optimized
LCOE for each ﬂuid, for power generation only, is calculated at 45% capacity factor, then used to
calculate the minimized LCOW for working ﬂuid qualities between x = 0 and x = 1, see Figures
32 and 33 for packed-bedrock TES.
Figure 32: LCOW for ﬂuids using Therminol 55.
Figure 33: LCOW for ﬂuids using rapeseed oil.
When optimizing for LCOW it is clear that the PEORC does not behave like a SCORC for all
ﬂuids, as most of the ﬂuids have their lowest LCOW at qualities between x = 0, 5 and x = 0, 9.
A low value of LCOW does not necessarily mean that the working ﬂuid is performing well, as the
LCOE must be evaluated as well. The trend for the two rapeseed oil storages is very similar, as is
the trend for the three Therminol 55 storages. Hence only the best storage conﬁguration for each
of the two HTFs is showed. The remaining graphs can be seen in Appendices B.1, B.2 and B.3.
The choice of working ﬂuid is again less signiﬁcant for the TLORC with rapeseed oil.
69
==PAGE:86==
3.3
Cogeneration
3.3.2
Therminol 55
The values for the levelized cost of water and levelized cost of electricity for the investigated
conﬁgurations with Therminol 55 are presented in this section. Each ﬂuids optimized LCOE for
power generation only has been used to calculate the optimized LCOW for cogeneration.
Packed-Bedrock Thermal Energy Storage
In Figure 34 the LCOW and LCOE for SCORC and PEORC using packed-bedrock TES with
Therminol 55 is shown. The TLORC is shown later with the other TLORCs, as their high values
of LCOE make it diﬃcult to determine the values for PEORC and SCORC.
Figure 34: LCOE vs LCOW for PEORC and SCORC for diﬀerent working ﬂuids using packed-
bedrock TES with Therminol 55.
The general trend is that a low LCOE results in a high LCOW. This is expected because the
subtracted LCOE · Ecogen in the numerator in Eq.(126) is smaller for a low LCOE, and thus a
higher investment cost is required for cogeneration when a system has a low LCOE. Figure 34
shows that the SCORC working ﬂuids generally have a lower LCOE and higher LCOW than the
PEORCs using the same working ﬂuid. MDM has a LCOE that is almost equal to the selling
price of electricity, and thus a LCOW much lower than the rest. It is diﬃcult to determine which
working ﬂuid is best from Figure 34, as it depends on the eﬀective net gain from selling electricity
and water. Therefore, the PBT is used to evaluate the performance of each conﬁguration. The
PBT for packed-bedrock TES using Therminol 55 is seen in Table 15.

storage technology using Therminol 55.

Trilateral Cycles
The LCOW vs LCOE for trilateral cycles for all storage technologies using Therminol 55 are seen
in Figure 37. The LCOEs are extremely high for the trilateral cycles, hence showing them in their
own diagram. It was expected that the LCOW would be lower for all conﬁgurations. Instead,

at the power generation only cycle. The condensation temperature for the trilateral cycle using
indirect storage is around 75 °C, which is not far from the cogeneration condensation temperature.
Therefore, the increase in capital cost is not signiﬁcant for the indirect storage when applying
cogeneration, thus resulting in a very low LCOW. Cogeneration is advantageous for around half of
the trilateral cycles, as they have a LCOW considerably below selling price. However, apart from
packed-bedrock TES with toluene, they are all still infeasible due to their high LCOEs.

==PAGE:96==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
3.4
Parametric Analyses
In this section diﬀerent vital parameters are investigated and analysed.
3.4.1
Condensation Temperature
With the implementation of cogeneration, the condensation temperature increases signiﬁcantly,
consequently reducing the pressure drop in the expander. Based on a study by K. Darvish et al.
[83], it was earlier determined that the pressure drop in the expander aﬀected the cost greatly. An
investigation of the condensation temperature and speciﬁc investment cost for power generation
only using SCORC packed-bedrock TES with Therminol is seen on Figure 42. The condensation
temperature is seen to increase the speciﬁc investment cost of the system substantially, as the ORC
circuit needs a much higher heat input in order to be able to keep generating 1 MW gross electricity.
The increased heat input includes larger component sizes, larger storage and larger solar collector
ﬁeld, which was concluded to account for the majority of cost. For cogeneration to be advantageous
the proﬁt of selling freshwater must compensate for the large increase in investment cost. This is
the case for some of the trilateral cycles, because a large amount of heat already was present in the
condenser during power generation only.
Figure 42: Condensation temperature’s inﬂuence on speciﬁc investment cost for SCORC packed-
bedrock TES using Therminol 55.
80
==PAGE:97==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
3.4.2
Levelized Cost of Water
Figure 43 shows the levelized cost of water’s inﬂuence on the pay-back period for the cogeneration
toluene-based TLORC using packed-bedrock TES with Therminol 55. It shows that the necessary
LCOW for TLORC to compete with the subcritical power only cycle is 0,68 €/m3, assuming the
optimized LCOE for the trilateral cycle. There are several parameters that aﬀect the LCOW such
as the capacity factor of the plant and selling prices of electricity and freshwater.
Figure 43: LCOW vs PBT for TLORC using packed-bedrock TES with Therminol 55.
3.4.3
Electricity Selling Price
The electricity selling price is a dynamic factor that changes throughout the day, depending on the
country’s market rules, share of renewables in the electricity grid and supply and demand. The
worldwide electricity prices vary greatly from country to country [94], and are frequently regulated
by politics and tariﬀs and adjusted based on the BNP pr. capita, hence electricity prices are often
higher for developed countries than developing countries [94]. Based on the location of the study,
the electricity price is varied between 0,1 €/kWh to 0,2 €/kWh.
81
==PAGE:98==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
Figure 44: Electricity selling price’s inﬂuence on payback-period for Therminol 55.
The impact the electricity price has on the pay-back period for the power only conﬁgurations with
Therminol 55 and rapeseed oil is seen in Figure 44 and 45, respectively. The direct and indirect
TES for the TLORCs with Therminol 55 are not visible in Figure 44 making them infeasible even
with an increase in electricity price. In Figure 45 both TLORC TES types with rapeseed oil are
shown to be within the feasible pay-back period of thirty years, due to the improvement of the
TLORC performance when using rapeseed oil.
The electricity price appears to inﬂuence the PBT greatly. The ﬁgures show similar curve trends, as
to be expected, since all the conﬁgurations have a ﬁxed gross electricity output of 1 MWe, and the
changes therefore happen uniformly. The small variation in trends is due to diﬀerent net electricity
outputs. The trilateral cycles for rapeseed oil appear to be extremely sensitive to the electricity
price, with an almost vertical downwards going curve. However, this can be due two reasons: the
TLORC rapeseed oil curves look like the other curves, but merely needs a higher electricity price
to be visible, and/or due to the minuscule data below the PBT of 30 years it makes it impossible
for the graphs to be curved. For Therminol 55 the curves are starting to ﬂat out, meaning a higher
electricity selling price shows less signiﬁcance to the PBT, which is also the case for rapeseed oil,
but at higher selling prices.
82
==PAGE:99==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
Figure 45: Electricity selling price’s inﬂuence on payback-period for rapeseed oil.
3.4.4
Freshwater Selling Price
The freshwater selling price varies greatly worldwide [93] and is often also inﬂuenced by political
decisions and supply and demand.
Therefore, the sensitivity of the freshwater selling price is
studied, where the selling price is varied from 1 €/m3 to 2 €/m3. The sensitivity of the freshwater
selling price for Therminol 55 and rapeseed oil conﬁgurations can be seen in Figure 46 and Figure
47, respectively.
Figure 46: Freshwater selling price’s inﬂuence on pay-back period for rapeseed oil storage conﬁgu-
rations.
83
==PAGE:100==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
Naturally, a higher selling price reduces the pay-back period. The trilateral cycle for 2T-indirect
TES is not feasible, even at a freshwater selling price of 2 €/m3. This is however not unexpected,
as the TLORC with indirect storage had the worst performance of all cases, because the minimum
safety margin temperature of the molten salt limits the temperature span of the trilateral cycle,
and thus eliminates all potential advantage from using a trilateral cycle. The selling price aﬀects
the PBT signiﬁcantly, with the trilateral cycle being aﬀected more than the subcritical cycle, as
the trilateral curve is steeper. Considering the trilateral cycles have more heat in their condenser,
and thereby generate more freshwater, it is expected that the freshwater selling price impacts the
trilateral cycles more.
Figure 47: Freshwater selling price’s inﬂuence on pay-back period for rapeseed oil storage conﬁgu-
rations.
Rapeseed oil storage based conﬁgurations are even more aﬀected by the freshwater selling price,
than the Therminol 55 based ones are. Seen on Figure 47 the curves are much steeper than for
Therminol 55, i.e. the pay-back period is more aﬀected by the freshwater selling price. This is
anticipated, because the rapeseed oil conﬁgurations have more heat in the condenser, due to a
lower thermal eﬃciency, caused by the lower turbine inlet temperature.
However, even at the
highest freshwater selling price the 2T-direct trilateral cycle is still not within the feasible PBT of
30 years.
84
==PAGE:101==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
3.4.5
Capacity Factor and Future Collectors
Foil-based CSP collectors are easier and cheaper to produce compared to mirror based CSP col-
lectors and an increase in production rate and usage of standard components ensure security of
supply. Literature [15] therefore suggests that future foil-based CSP collectors have a high cost
reduction potential. Consequently, the future cost for foil-based concentrated solar panels are in-
vestigated, considering a 20% heat loss coeﬃcient reduction and costs of 150 €/m2 and 135 €/m2
for Therminol 55 and rapeseed oil based collectors [15], respectively. The capacity factor of a plant
is based on location and plant type. In a study by U. Herrmann et al. [56], located in Barstow,
California, the capacity factor was investigated based on the storage capacity for PTC, where a
single-axis-tracking PTC integrated ORC plant with 9 hour TES capacity has a capacity factor of
50%. Therefore, the capacity factor is investigated at 45%, 50% and 55% on the best performing
thermal energy storage technology, packed-bedrock, for current and future collectors. The capacity
factor’s inﬂuence on PBT for packed-bedrock storage using Therminol 55 can be seen in Figure
48. The electricity selling price is 0,15 €/kWh and the freshwater selling price is 1,5 €/m3. The
trilateral cycles are blue, the subcritical cycles are red, the current collectors are the straight lines,
the future collectors are the dotted lines and the lines marked with triangles are for cogeneration.
Figure 48: Capacity factor’s inﬂuence on pay-back period for packed-bedrock storage using Ther-
minol 55.
The PBT is greatly aﬀected by the capacity factor, as the PBT decreases approximately 30% from
a capacity factor increase of 45% to 55%. Additionally, the PBT is also signiﬁcantly decreased when
using the future collectors, as to be expected, considering it was earlier concluded that the solar
85
==PAGE:102==
3.4
Parametric Analyses
ﬁeld accounted for more than 50% of the total capital investment. Naturally, a signiﬁcant reduction
in cost, as well as higher solar eﬃciency due to lower heat loss, leads to an improvement of the
system. Speciﬁcally the trilateral cycles beneﬁt from using the future collectors, as it is proven that
the trilateral cycles have a higher heat input, and therefore also solar ﬁeld, consequently having
a larger relative impact. Moreover, the cogeneration trilateral cycles beneﬁt even more, and are
in fact almost competitive with the subcritical cycles using future collectors. Figure 49 shows the
capacity factor’s inﬂuence on PBT for packed-bedrock storage using rapeseed oil.
Figure 49: Capacity factor’s inﬂuence on pay-back period for packed-bedrock storage using rapeseed
oil.
Similar trends are occurring for rapeseed oil as for Therminol 55. Future collectors are even more
signiﬁcant for rapeseed oil conﬁgurations, because larger heat inputs are required due to a lower
heat source temperature and consequently lower thermal eﬃciency [90]. The TLORC for power
only with current collectors is not visible on the diagram, as it is never feasible. At capacity factor
45% the subcritical cogeneration cycle with future collectors performs better than the subcritical
power cycle with future collectors, however the subcritical power cycle performs better at higher
capacity factors. On the contrary, the trilateral cogeneration cycle with future collectors performs
much better than the trilateral power generation only cycle, and is nearly as good as the subcritical
cycles with future collectors, which is a signiﬁcant increase from using the current collectors. As
beforementioned, this is due to the trilateral cycles having a larger solar collector area, and therefore
beneﬁt more from cheaper collectors. It must thus be concluded that future collectors beneﬁt the
trilateral cycles more than the subcritical cycles.
86
==PAGE:103==
4
Discussion
In this study the partial evaporation organic Rankine cycle is compared with the conventional
subcritical organic Rankine cycle for small- to medium-scale solar thermal applications, with a
ﬁxed power output of 1 MWe. In addition, three diﬀerent thermal energy storage technologies were
analysed and compared to increase the capacity factor of the plant. Various working ﬂuids have
been evaluated through an optimization routine implemented in MATLAB.
The current study found that the SCORC has a lower investment cost and energy production
cost than the PEORC for medium-temperature solar applications. With optimized ﬂuid quality
the PEORC behaved like a SCORC, with an evaporator outlet ﬂuid quality of 100%. Therefore,
the trilateral organic Rankine cycle was investigated in addition to the partial evaporated organic
Rankine cycle. The results of this study furthermore show that implementing a multi-eﬀect distil-
lation system for cogeneration is advantageous for most of the TLORCs but not for the SCORCs.
Considering the quantity of heat in the condenser for the trilateral cycles it was expected that the
usage of this heat would beneﬁt the trilateral cycles more. These results are associated with a study
by Hays et al. [76] that concludes that trilateral cycles have a higher heat input and net power
output than subcritical cycles. Conﬁgurations with lower thermal eﬃciency have higher condenser
waste heat without cogeneration. This is furthermore consistent with a study by J. Fischer [99]
that found the thermal eﬃciency of the trilateral cycles to always be lower than the subcritical
cycle, considering the same system. With no thermal losses included in the present study, elec-
tricity output ﬁxed and lower thermal eﬃciency, naturally more heat is left in the condenser for
the trilateral cycles. Therefore, cogeneration can increase the revenues for some of the trilateral
cycle based systems and make some of them economically feasible, but never competitive with the
SCORC using same conditions investigated in this study.
Many of the PBT values found in this study were not feasible, as they exceeded the 30 years
lifetime of the project. When considering the selling prices of electricity and heat are 0,15 €/kWh
and 1,5 €/m3, respectively, and furthermore the LCOE and LCOW of 0,13 €/kWh and 1,49 €/m3,
respectively, from the best performing cogeneration conﬁguration, it is expected that the pay-
back periods are long. These results are in line with those of a previous study by Desai et al.
[15], where the LCOE and LCOW were found to be 0,141 €/kWh and 1,42 €/m3, respectively,
however not including pay-back periods and hence no selling price of electricity or freshwater. If
the same quantity and electricity and freshwater selling prices were used as in the present study,
similar pay-back periods would occur. These values are in agreement with those obtained by I.
Baniasad et al.
[85], where LCOE and LCOW were found to be 0,16 €/kWh and 1,38 €/m3,
respectively, for a solar ORC using a 7.5 hour thermal storage and MED system to generate
freshwater. In the present study it was found that the LCOE and LCOW and consequently pay-
back periods are very high compared to large-scale fossil fueled centralised power plants, that can
87
==PAGE:104==
reach pay-back periods as low as a couple of years [100] and LCOE as low as 0,02 €/kWh [101].
However, the environmental factors must also come into play. It is expected that the LCOE is
higher for renewables than the dispatchable fossil fuel based power plants, which is why renewable
energy sources are often supported by governments’ monetary incentives.
Furthermore, large-
scale centralised production is expected to be signiﬁcantly cheaper than small- to medium-scale
decentralised production. However, electricity is still a necessity in vastly decentralised locations,
and despite the long pay-back periods, it may still be a better investment than extending the
centralised electricity grid. Therefore, when comparing the performances in the present study it is
necessary to consider technologies that are usable in decentralised locations. An extensively used
decentralised technology for power production is solar photovoltaics. However, the diﬃculty of
eﬃcient storage of electricity is a problem, considering the non-dispatchable nature of renewables.
Patil et al. [102] compared solar ORC to solar PV in northern Chile and found that the energy
loss of a battery storage from a solar PV was signﬁcantly higher than the energy loss in a thermal
energy storage from a solar thermal. It was furthermore concluded that solar integrated ORC oﬀers
reliable decentralized power supply, due to the cost-eﬀectiveness of the thermal storage. Patil et al.
also concluded that due to the reliability of the solar ORC, the solar ORC’s LCOE of 0,16 €/kWh
is competitive with the solar PV’s LCOE of 0,10 €/kWh. In the study [102] annual simulations
were run, which gave a capacity factor of 56% for a 9 hour thermal energy storage. Considering
the capacity factor found by Patil et al. and the present study’s ideal location for solar energy, it
is possible that the assumed capacity factor of 45% for the systems in the present study is too low,
hence investigated the inﬂuence of capacity factors of 50% and 55%. The capacity factor proved
to be a very signiﬁcant parameter, that aﬀects the pay-back period, LCOE and LCOW greatly.
Therefore, these parameters should be interpreted with caution, when annual simulations are not
run. It would be interesting, for future work of the present study, to run annual simulations where
part-load performances, thermal losses and varying DNI and ambient temperatures were included,
to ﬁnd the real life capacity factor and performance of the system.
The packed-bedrock thermal energy storage proved to be the cheapest thermal energy storage for
both heat transfer ﬂuids and ORCs, as it allowed the beneﬁts of a direct storage but with a lower
investment cost. The performance of the TLORC with indirect storage is severely limited by the
high temperature restrictions that molten salt requires. The inlet temperature of the solar ﬁeld
for the TLORC and SCORC with indirect storage is hence 165°C, thus eliminating the TLORC’s
advantage of being able to achieve a lower outlet temperature of the heat source in the evaporator.
Consequently, the condensation temperature is 75 °C for the trilateral indirect storage, which
may be due to the large temperature diﬀerence between the heat source outlet and working ﬂuid
inlet.
This diﬀerence would be even larger, if the condensation temperature was lower.
The
indirect storage for the trilateral cycle does not make sense to investigate, as it limits the TLORC.
Nevertheless, the indirect storage is not cheaper than the packed-bedrock storage, and the results
88
==PAGE:105==
are therefore not aﬀected by the high condensation temperature present in the trilateral indirect
storage.
In this work, the same expander correlations have been used for the SCORC and PEORC architec-
ture as appropriate correlations for two-phase expansion cannot be obtained outside of companies.
The overall isentropic eﬃciency of the expander decreases due to the moisture content that is present
in a wet expansion [82]. The addition of these correlations will hence decrease the performance of
the PEORC.
The optimum pinch-point temperature for nearly every TLORC conﬁgurations in this thesis has
been between 10°C to 30°C, which is very high in comparison to diﬀerent studies.
In a study
from Lai et al. [91] it was shown that for low-temperature waste heat sources the best pinch-point
temperature is 5°C for both TLORCs and SCORCs. In order for the TLORC to fully exploit its heat
recovery potential the pinch-point temperature should be as low as possible but this is not the case
with a medium-temperature heat source. Furthermore, the temperature proﬁles in the evaporator
for the TLORC should be parallel connected to extract as much heat as possible from the heat
source. This trend is not shown for any of the cycles with a heat carrier inlet temperature of 278°C
and the TLORC design is hence not advantageous under these conditions. The usage of rapeseed
oil also shows a clear improvement for the TLORC because the heat carrier inlet temperature is
reduced to 220°C.
Lai et al. [91] showed that the high exergy eﬃciency for a TLORC is more inﬂuential than the
high thermal eﬃciency of a SCORC under low-temperature heat source conditions. However, in
this thesis the opposite has been discovered for medium-temperature heat source conditions. The
exergy eﬃciency of the SCORC increases drastically if the heat source inlet temperature is increased
thus removing the beneﬁt of a PEORC. This discovery is supported by the results from a study
by Fischer [99] where the total exergy eﬃciency is slightly larger for the SCORC than the TLORC
with a heat carrier inlet temperature of 280°C and a cooling agent inlet temperature of 62°C.
These ﬁndings also align with the results from a study by Lecompte et al. [2] where the TLORC
only shows a performance improvement over the SCORC at low temperatures. Therefore, it is
recommended for future work to investigate the performance diﬀerence between the PEORC and
SCORC at low-temperature conditions and compare it with the results from medium-temperature
conditions. Using CSP for PEORC architecture does not make sense, as the the CSP temperatures
are simply too high for the PEORC to be advantageous. It should be noted that the advantage
of having a lower heat source mass ﬂow rate, that occurs for the PEORC, is not considered. This
includes smaller pipes, smaller pipe heat losses and overall smaller heat source dimensioning, which
would all favour the feasibility of the PEORC. However, only a small change is expected from this.
The present study found that the partial evaporation organic Rankine cycle with foil-based CSP
for medium-temperature solar applications is not competitive with the subcritical organic Rankine
89
==PAGE:106==
cycle, due to the high heat source temperature. Furthermore, cogeneration was proved to have a
positive performance inﬂuence for most of the trilateral cycles.
90
==PAGE:107==
5
Conclusions
The research includes a techno-economic comparison of the partial evaporation organic Rankine
cycle and the conventional subcritical organic Rankine cycle. The foil-based medium-temperature
concentrated solar integrated ORCs have various working ﬂuids and thermal energy storages com-
pared. Moreover, cogeneration was added to the cycle to desalinate saltwater in an attempt to
increase the performance of the project. It was found that the subcritical organic Rankine cycle
outperforms the partial evaporation organic Rankine cycle for the medium-temperature solar ap-
plication. With free working ﬂuid quality, the partial evaporation organic Rankine cycle behaved
like a subcritical cycle, therefore the trilateral organic Rankine cycle was investigated. The best
performing conﬁguration, cyclopentane-based subcritical cycle with packed-bedrock storage using
Therminol 55, resulted in a LCOE of 0,12 €/kWh and a pay-back period of 20 years. It was further-
more discovered that the addition of cogeneration did not beneﬁt the subcritical organic Rankine
cycle, but it did however beneﬁt most of the trilateral organic Rankine cycles. Nevertheless, the best
performing cogeneration conﬁguration was still the subcritical cycle with packed-bedrock storage
using Therminol 55, but it is instead toluene based, resulting in a LCOE and LCOW of 0,13 €/kWh
and 1,49 €/m3, respectively, with a payback-period of 23 years. The best performing trilateral cycle
is also toluene based with packed-bedrock storage using Therminol 55, with a LCOE and LCOW
of 0,18 €/kWh and 0,80 €/m3, respectively, resulting in a pay-back period of 27 years. Therminol
55 proved to be a better heat transfer ﬂuid, due to higher turbine inlet temperatures leading to
higher thermal eﬃciencies. The cheaper and lower temperature of rapeseed oil was found to be
better for the 2T-direct storage and the trilateral cycles, for power generation only. The savings
from the cheaper heat transfer ﬂuid was clear in cases with large quantity of heat transfer ﬂuid in
the storage as well as in conﬁgurations with large heat inputs.
It is seen that the exergy eﬃciency for the power production remains nearly the same for both the
subcritical and partial evaporation cycle, except with an indirect TES, where the SCORC had a
higher exergy eﬃciency due to the molten salt temperature limitation. For the best performing
conﬁguration the exergy eﬃciencies for the power production of the TLORC and SCORC are 48%
and 43%, respectively. Therefore, it can be concluded that the PEORC does not have its usual
exergetic advantage over the SCORC with medium-temperature solar applications, thus performing
worse.
The electricity and heat prices were found to have a tremendous aﬀect on the pay-back time of
the cycles, and were therefore also part of the reason why many of the conﬁgurations proved to
be infeasible, with a pay-back period exceeding the project’s lifetime of 30 years. This was also
visible on the diﬀerent conﬁgurations’ values of LCOE and LCOW, where many had both LCOE
and LCOW higher than the selling prices.
The storages using rapeseed oil and the trilateral
cycles proved to be more aﬀected by the freshwater selling price, since more heat is present in the
91
==PAGE:108==
condenser.
Cogeneration and trilateral cycles were found to have signiﬁcant advantage from using future col-
lectors. The reason for that is the trilateral cycles and cogeneration have considerably higher heat
inputs than the subcritical cycles with power generation only. From using future collectors it was
found that the trilateral cycle with cogeneration was nearly competitive with the subcritical cycle
with power generation only.
In the present study a capacity factor of 45% was assumed, which showed to have tremendous aﬀect
on the pay-back time. In a similar study at the same location, where annual simulations were run,
the capacity factor was found to be 56%. This leads to the conclusion that the assumed capacity
factor of 45% may have been too low. Annual simulations are required to be run for the system in
order to ﬁnd the real capacity factor and performance of the system, but it is not expected, based
on this study and literature, that the trilateral cycle will be competitive to the subcritical cycle for
medium-temperature CSP collectors.
The techno-economic analysis of the partial evaporation organic Rankine cycle found that the
subcritical organic Rankine cycle outperforms the partial evaporation cycle for solar thermal ap-
plications with a medium-temperature heat source.

B.4
MATLAB Code
In the following section the MATLAB codes are included. The ﬁrst code (DesignORC_General_Solar)
includes the inputs and optimization variables.
The next code (Optfun_general is the optimization code.
DesignFun_General prints the optimized values from the optimization code.
Solar_bedrock, Solar_direct and Solar_indirect are the three storage codes.
Turbeﬀis the turbine eﬃciency code for the conventional turbine expander.
Turbeﬀ-screw is the turbine eﬃciency code for the screw expanders.
Result.geo contains various results when the code has been run.
Computecost is the cost correlation code.
107

%% Thermo-economic design and optimization of ORC unit
% This code was developed by Roberto Pili at the Technical University of
% Munich in 2015 and should not be shared with others if not authorized
 
 
clear all
clc
close all
 
t_init = cputime;
tic
 
%% Define cycle architecture
% PEORC; SCORC; 'comparison' = comparison between PEORC and SCORC
% PEORC --> evaporator outlet quality ranges between 0 and 1
% SCORC --> evaporator outlet quality ranges between 1 and 3
% comparison --> evaporator outlet quality ranges between 0 and 3
 
cycle_architecture_set=["PEORC";"SCORC";"comps"];
 
for  r=1:1
    cycle_architecture=cycle_architecture_set(r);
    if cycle_architecture=='PEORC'
        xout_wf_eva_max = 0; % Max value of quality at evaporator outlet
        xout_wf_eva_min = 0; % Min value of quality at evaporator outlet
    elseif cycle_architecture=='SCORC'
        xout_wf_eva_max = 2; % Max value of quality at evaporator outlet
        xout_wf_eva_min = 1; % Min value of quality at evaporator outl et
    elseif cycle_architecture=='comps'
        xout_wf_eva_max = 2; % Max value of quality at evaporator outlet
        xout_wf_eva_min = 0; % Min value of quality at evaporator outlet
    end
     
    %% Define set of inputs
    % Heat source minimum outlet temperature
    n_out_hs_lim=1; % Number of investigated minimum outlet temperatures of the heat 
source between upper and lower bound
    %Change min. limit temperature to something low (30) when direct and bedrock & to 165
    %when indirect
    Tout_hs_lim_ub=30+273.15; % Upper bound of the set of minimum outlet temperatures of 
the heat source in K
    Tout_hs_lim_lb=30+273.15; % Lower bound of the set of minimum outlet temperatures of 
the heat source in K
    Tout_hs_lim_set=linspace(Tout_hs_lim_lb,Tout_hs_lim_ub,n_out_hs_lim); % Input set of 
inlet temperatures of the heat source in K
    % Heat source inlet temperature (278 C for therminol and 220 C for rapeseed oil)
    n_in_hs=1; % Number of investigated inlet temperatures of the heat source between the 
upper bound and lower bound
    Tin_hs_ub=278+273.15; % Upper bound of the set of inlet temperatures of the heat 
source in K
    Tin_hs_lb=278+273.15; % Lower bound of the set of inlet temperatures of the heat 
==PAGE:125==
28-06-21 15:57
C:\MATLAB\DesignORC_General_solar.m
2 of 10
source in K
    Tin_hs_set=linspace(Tin_hs_lb,Tin_hs_ub,n_in_hs); % Input set of inlet temperatures 
of the heat source in K
    % Fluid selection
    %Medium_set=["cyclopen","toluene","EBENZENE","MM","MDM","Chlorobenzene","heptane","
hexane"]; % Reduced list of fluids
    Medium_set=["toluene"];
    n_fluid=length(Medium_set);
       
    % Heat source design conditions
    for l=1:n_out_hs_lim
        Tout_hs_lim = Tout_hs_lim_set(l);  % Minimum allowed temperature of heat source 
in K
        for i=1:n_in_hs
            Tin_hs = Tin_hs_set(i); % Inlet temperature of heat source in K
            if Tin_hs <= Tout_hs_lim
                break
            else
 
                Tm = 0.5*(Tin_hs + Tout_hs_lim);   
                P_eva_hs = 700; %...(Evaporation pressure in kPa plus 4.5 kPa safety for 
Therminol_55 at 278 deg C=doesn't matter )
               
                
 
               % Therminol 55 data
               therminol_prop=readmatrix('therminol_55_T_rho_cp');
               Therminol_T=therminol_prop(:,1);
               Therminol_rho=therminol_prop(:,2);
               Therminol_cp=therminol_prop(:,3);
               cpm_hs=interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_cp,Tm,'spline','extrap');
               
                Dp_p_hs=200; % Pressure drop in hot source circuit in kPa
             
                
                % Heat sink conditions
                %Tin_cs = 20+273.15; % Inlet temperature of cold source in K (for power)
                Tin_cs = 50+273.15; %inlet temperature of cold source in K (for cogen)
                Dp_f_cs=200; % Pressure drop covered by the cold source fan in Pa
                cpm_cold = refpropm('c', 't', Tin_cs, 'p', 1e2, 'Water'); % Average 
specific heat of water in J/kgK
                
                SIC_vector=ones(n_fluid); % Initial vector of SIC for different fluids
                Q_in_vector=ones(n_fluid); % Initial vector of Pnet for different fluids
                xout_eva_wf_vector=ones(n_fluid); % Initial vector of xout_eva_wf for 
different fluids
                
                % Working fluid
                for  j=1:n_fluid
                    Medium = Medium_set{j}; % Working fluid

3 of 10
                    %% Thermodynamic design - assumptions
                    dTpp_min = 5; % Minimum pinch-point temperature difference in K
                    dTpp_max = 100; % Maximum pinch-point temperature difference in K
                    dTpp_k_min = 5; % Minimum pinch-point temperature difference in K
                    dTpp_k_max = 50; % Maximum pinch-point temperature difference in K
                    
                    Tmax=refpropm('T','M',10e2,'T',100+273.15,Medium); % Refprop max 
temperature for the working fluid in K
                    T_triple_wf=refpropm('T','R',10e2,'T',100+273.15,Medium); % Triple 
point temperature of working fluid in K
                    pc_wf = refpropm('P', 'C',10e2, 'T', 100+273.15, Medium); % Critical 
pressure of working fluid in kPa
                    Tc_wf = refpropm('T', 'C',10e2, 'T', 100+273.15, Medium); % Critical 
temperature of working fluid in K
                    p_triple_wf=refpropm('P','R',10e2,'T',100+273.15,Medium); % Triple 
point pressure of working fluid in K
                    
                    T_wf_k_min=Tin_cs + dTpp_k_min; % Minimum condensing temperature of 
working fluid in K
                    Tout_wf_eva_max = min(Tin_hs-dTpp_min, Tmax-1); % Maximum evaporator 
outlet temperature of working fluid in K
 
                    T_wf_k_max =min(373.15,min(0.95*Tmax,0.95*Tc_wf)); % Maximum 
condensing temperature of working fluid in KK
                    Tout_wf_eva_min = Tout_hs_lim+dTpp_min; % Minimal evaporator outlet 
temperature of working fluid in K
                                                          
                    p_wf_eva_min = max(p_triple_wf*1.1,1.1*refpropm('P', 'T', 
Tout_wf_eva_min, 'Q', 0, Medium)); % Minimum evaporation pressure of working fluid in kPa
                    p_wf_eva_max = min(0.8*pc_wf,refpropm('P','T',min(Tout_wf_eva_max,0.9
*Tc_wf),'Q',0,Medium)); % maximum evaporation pressure of working fluid in kPa
           
                    % Definition of a first attempt value for the evaporation pressure 
that
                    % avoids numerical problems (negative heat exchange in the 
evaporator)
                    T_eva_wf_average = 0.4*Tin_hs+0.6*Tout_hs_lim;
                    Tc_wf=refpropm('T', 'C',10e2, 'T', 100+273.15, Medium); % Critical 
temperature for working fluid in K
                    T_eva_wf_0=min(0.9*Tc_wf,T_eva_wf_average);% First attempt value for 
the evaporation temperature in K
                    p_eva_wf_0 = refpropm('P', 'T',T_eva_wf_0, 'Q', 0, Medium); % First 
attempt value for the evaporation pressure in kPa
                    x0_p=(p_eva_wf_0-p_wf_eva_min)/(p_wf_eva_max-p_wf_eva_min);        
                    
                    eta_p_hs = 0.75; % Heat source pump isentropic efficiency
                    eta_pm_hs = 0.95; % Heat source pump motor efficiency
                    eta_pi_hs = 0.98; % Heat source pump inverter efficiency
                    eta_p = 0.70; % ORC pump isentropic efficiency
                    eta_pm = 0.95; % ORC pump motor efficiency
                    eta_pi = 0.98; % ORC pump inverter efficiency

                    Pen = 0.2; %penalty for PEORC on turbine efficiency
                    eta_e_PEORC = 0.75; % Expander efficiency for PEORC - not used
                    eta_e_SCORC = 0.85; % Expander efficiency for SCORC - not used
                    eta_e=[eta_e_PEORC;eta_e_SCORC]; % Expander efficiency

                    eta_f_cs = 0.75; % Cooling source fan isentropic efficiency

                    Const_CL_C = 0;     % Collector efficiency constant C

                    %C_CL_Sp = 135;      %Cost of future collectors rapeseed oil
                    C_Land_Sp = 4.7;    % Cost of land "€/m2"
                    Land_req = 3.5;     % Required land pr area (land_req * A)
                    
                    %% Maximum inlet effect from the heat source
                    eta_th_min = 1 - T_wf_k_max/Tout_wf_eva_min;  %Minimum thermal 
efficiency
                    P_e = 1*10^6; %Electric power required in W
                    
                    Q_max = P_e / (eta_g * eta_th_min); %Maximum heat rate into the 
system
 
                    %% Storage inputs
                    L_CT_ini = 0.85; %Initial level of the cold tank
                    L_HT_ini = 0.15; %Initial level of the hot tank 
                    t_st = 8; %Hours of storage capacity 
                    
                    dT_min_ST = 5; %Minimum temperature difference between hot source and 
tank 
                    effectiveV = 0.7; %Ratio of usable volume compared to the actual 
volume
                    
                    %% Target function
                    goal = 'LCOW'; % 1: LCOE (minimum levelized cost of electricity), 2: 
A_CL (minimum inlet heat rate), 3: SIC (minimum specific investment cost for the ORC

power system), 4: NPV (maximum net present value), 5: PBT (minimum pay-back time), 6:
C_inv_tot (minimum total capital investment), 7:LCOW (levelized cost of water)
                    compCosts = 1; % if costs should be calculated
                    
                    if p_wf_eva_max <= 100
                        continue
                    end
                     
                    %% Economic factors
                    interest = 0.03; % Interest rate
                    N_op = 30; % Ammortization time in years
                    cap_factor = 0.45; %capacity factor
                    h_year = 8760*cap_factor; %
                    C_om=0.02; % O&M fixed cost in percentage of the investment cost per 
year
                    MI=0.0; % Monetary incentive per produced energy per year in €/kWh
                    E_price=0.15; % Selling price of electricity in €/kWh
                    Heat_price = 1.5; %selling price of fresh water in €/m3 (reference

function [z] = optFun_General(in, Tout_hs_lim, cpm_hs, Medium, eta_p_hs, eta_pm_hs, 
eta_pi_hs, Dp_p_hs, eta_f_cs, eta_pm_cs, eta_pi_cs, Dp_f_cs, eta_p, eta_pm, eta_g, 
p_wf_eva_max, p_wf_eva_min, Tout_wf_eva_max,xout_wf_eva_max, xout_wf_eva_min, dTpp_max, 
dTpp_min, Tin_cs, dTpp_k_max, dTpp_k_min, T_wf_k_max,T_wf_k_min,Q_max, goal, Tin_hs,
eta_pi,n_e_stages,interest, N_op,h_year,C_om,MI,E_price, compCosts,P_eva_hs,P_e,I_D,
Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,T_a, Tm,C_CL_Sp,C_Land_Sp,Land_req,dT_min_ST,t_st,
L_CT_ini,L_HT_ini,effectiveV,Pen,Heat_price,Tc_wf)
 
% Input vector in absolute terms
p_wf_eva = (in(1)*(p_wf_eva_max-p_wf_eva_min)+p_wf_eva_min); % evaporation pressure of 
working fluid in kPa
xout_wf_eva = in(2)*(xout_wf_eva_max-xout_wf_eva_min)+xout_wf_eva_min; % evaporator 
outlet temperature of working fluid in K
rec_eff = in(3); % recuperator effectiveness
dTpp = in(4)*(dTpp_max-dTpp_min)+dTpp_min; % pinch-point temperature difference at 
evaporator in K
dTpp_k = in(5)*(dTpp_k_max-dTpp_k_min)+dTpp_k_min; % pinch-point temperature difference 
at condenser in K
T_wf_k = in(6)*(T_wf_k_max - T_wf_k_min)+T_wf_k_min; % condensing temperature in K
Tin_hs = min(Tin_hs) + in(7)*(min(Tin_hs) - min(Tin_hs)); % inlet temperature of heat 
source in K
p_wf_k = refpropm('P', 'T', T_wf_k, 'Q', 0, Medium); % condensing pressure in kPa
 
%% Cycle calculations
[h_wf_eva_L, T_wf_eva_L] = refpropm('HT','P',p_wf_eva,'Q',0,Medium); % temperature in K 
and specific enthalpy in J/kg at sat. liquid
[h_wf_eva_V, T_wf_eva_V] = refpropm('HT','P',p_wf_eva,'Q',1,Medium); % temperature in K 
and specific enthalpy in J/kg at sat. vapour
hout_wf_eva = h_wf_eva_L + xout_wf_eva*(h_wf_eva_V - h_wf_eva_L); % specific enthalpy in 
J/kg for working fluid at evaporator outlet
hout_wf_eva_max = refpropm('H','M',10e2,'T',100+273.15,Medium); % Maximum evaporator 
outlet enthalpy of working fluid in J/kg
 
if hout_wf_eva > hout_wf_eva_max
    if strcmp(goal, 'LCOE')
        z = 1e5;
    elseif strcmp(goal, 'A_CL')
        z = 1e15;
    elseif strcmp(goal, 'SIC')
        z = 1e5;
    elseif strcmp(goal, 'NPV')
        z = 1e15;
    elseif strcmp(goal, 'PBT')
        z = 1e5;
    elseif strcmp(goal,'C_inv_tot')
        z = 1e15;        
    elseif strcmp(goal,'LCOW')
        z = 1e15; 
    end
    return
end

if xout_wf_eva>0 %if no TLORC a single pinch point is present
if Tout_wf_eva > Tout_wf_eva_max
    if strcmp(goal, 'LCOE')

for working fluid in K

evaporator output for working fluid in J/kgK
sout_wf_eva_max = refpropm('S','M',10e2,'T',100+273.15,Medium); % Maximum evaporator 
outlet entropy of working fluid in J/kgK
 
[hin_wf_p, sin_wf_p] = refpropm('HS','T',T_wf_k,'Q',0,Medium); % specific enthalpy in 
J/kg and specific entropy in J/kgK of working fluid at pump inlet
hout_wf_ps = refpropm('H','P',p_wf_eva,'S',sin_wf_p,Medium); % specific enthalpy of 
working fluid at pump outlet in J/kg (isentropic)
hout_wf_p = hin_wf_p + (hout_wf_ps-hin_wf_p)/eta_p; % specific enthalpy of working fluid 
at pump outlet in J/kg
Tout_wf_p = refpropm('T','H',hout_wf_p,'P',p_wf_eva,Medium); % temperature of working 
fluid at pump outlet in K

b=0;

    hout_wf_rec_k = hout_wf_e - qflow_rec;

    Tout_wf_rec_k = Tout_wf_e;
    Tout_wf_rec_eva = Tout_wf_p;
    qflow_rec = 0;
    hout_wf_rec_eva = hout_wf_p + qflow_rec;
    hout_wf_rec_k = hout_wf_e - qflow_rec;
    if Tout_wf_rec_eva > T_wf_eva_L-5 || Tout_wf_rec_k < T_wf_k-1
        if strcmp(goal, 'LCOE')
            z = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'A_CL')
            z = 1e15;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'SIC')
            z = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'NPV')
            z = 1e15;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'PBT')
            z = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal,'C_inv_tot')
            z = 1e15;   
        elseif strcmp(goal,'LCOW')
            z = 1e15; 
        end
        return
    end
end
 
% Differentiate how mass flow rate of the heat source is computed between
% PEORC and SCORC
if  hout_wf_eva <= h_wf_eva_L % For PEORC TRILATERAL architecture (x<=0)
    Tout_hs_eva = Tout_wf_rec_eva + dTpp; % Pinch point at cold section of preheating
    mflow_hs = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva-hout_wf_rec_eva)/(cpm_hs*(Tin_hs-Tout_hs_eva));
    Qflow_eva_LV=0;
    T_hs_eva_L = Tin_hs ; % heat source temperature at evaporator zone with sat. liquid 
in K
    Qflow_LtoOut = 0; % heat rate from sat. L to outlet for working fluid in W
    T_hs_eva_V = Tin_hs; % temperature of heat source at evaporator sat. V in K
    Qflow_eva_intoL = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva - hout_wf_rec_eva); % preheating heat rate at 
evaporator in W
else % For PEORC with x>0 and SCORC architectures
    T_hs_eva_L = T_wf_eva_L + dTpp;
    mflow_hs = -mflow_wf*(-hout_wf_eva+h_wf_eva_L)/(cpm_hs*(Tin_hs-T_hs_eva_L));
    Qflow_eva_intoL = mflow_wf*(h_wf_eva_L - hout_wf_rec_eva); % preheating heat rate at 
evaporator in W
    Tout_hs_eva=T_hs_eva_L-Qflow_eva_intoL/(mflow_hs*cpm_hs);
    Qflow_LtoOut = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva-h_wf_eva_L); % heat rate from sat. L to outlet 
for working fluid in W
    Qflow_eva_LV = mflow_wf*(min(h_wf_eva_V, hout_wf_eva) - h_wf_eva_L); % latent heat 
rate at evaporator in W

    if Tout_hs_eva-Tout_wf_rec_eva < dTpp % This means that the pinch point is at the 
wf_rec_eva/out_hs_eva section
        Tout_hs_eva=Tout_wf_rec_eva +dTpp;

coefficient in W/m2K at recuperator
        TD_HT = Tout_wf_e - Tout_wf_rec_eva; % high temperature difference in K 
(counterflow) at recuperator
        TD_LT = Tout_wf_rec_k - Tout_wf_p; % low temperature difference in K 
(counterflow) at recuperator
        LMTD_rec = (TD_HT - TD_LT)/log(TD_HT/TD_LT); % logarithmic temperature difference 
in K(counterflow) at recuperator
        A_rec = Qflow_rec/(U_rec*LMTD_rec); % required heat transfer area in m2 at 
recuperator
    else
        A_rec = 0;
    end
    
    % Condenser
    U_wf_dsh = 1400; % RP:1400 heat transfer coefficient of working fluid in W/m2K at 
condenser (desuperheating)
    U_wf_k_LV = 1600; % RP:1600 heat transfer coefficient of working fluid in W/m2K at 
condenser (two-phase)
    f_wf_k = 0.00018; % fouling resistance of working fluid in m2K/W at condenser
    A_ratio_wf_k = 1; % (4 in enhancing factor) area ratio of working fluid with respect 
to bare outer area at condenser
    
    U_cw_k = 4000/0.78; % RP:351.5/3 heat transfer coefficient of cooling air in W/m2K at 
condenser
    f_cw_k = 0.0004; % RP:0.00018 fouling resistance of cooling air in m2K/W at condenser
    A_ratio_cw_k = 0.78; % RP:3 area ratio of cooling air with respect to bare outer area 
at condenser
    Fk = 1; % where does this value come from?? correction factor for logarithmic 
temperature difference at condenser
    
    if Qflow_rec > 0
        U_dsh = 1/(1/(U_cw_k*A_ratio_cw_k) + f_cw_k/A_ratio_cw_k + 1/
(U_wf_dsh*A_ratio_wf_k) + f_wf_k/A_ratio_wf_k); % global heat transfer coefficient in 
W/m2K at condenser (desuperheating)
        TD_HT_dsh = Tout_wf_rec_k  - Tout_cs; % high temperature difference in K 
(counterflow) at condenser (desuperheating)
        TD_LT_dsh = T_wf_k - T_cs_V; % low temperature difference in K (counterflow) at 
condenser (desuperheating)
        LMTD_dsh = (TD_HT_dsh - TD_LT_dsh)/log(TD_HT_dsh/TD_LT_dsh); % logarithmic 
temperature difference in K(counterflow) at condenser (desuperheating)
        A_dsh =  Qflow_k_dsh/(U_dsh*LMTD_dsh*Fk); % required heat transfer area in m2 at 
condenser (desuperheating)
    else
        A_dsh = 0;
    end
    
    U_k_LV = 1/(1/(U_cw_k*A_ratio_cw_k) + f_cw_k/A_ratio_cw_k + 1/
(U_wf_k_LV*A_ratio_wf_k) + f_wf_k/A_ratio_wf_k); % global heat transfer coefficient in 
W/m2K at condenser (desuperheating)
    TD_HT_k_LV = T_wf_k - T_cs_V; % high temperature difference in K (counterflow) at 
==PAGE:148==
28-06-21 16:11
C:\MATLAB\optFun_General.m
15 of 17
condenser (desuperheating)
    TD_LT_k_LV = T_wf_k - Tin_cs; % low temperature difference in K (counterflow) at 
condenser (desuperheating)
    LMTD_k_LV = (TD_HT_k_LV - TD_LT_k_LV)/log(TD_HT_k_LV/TD_LT_k_LV); % logarithmic 
temperature difference in K(counterflow) at condenser (desuperheating)
    A_k_LV = (Qflow_k_LV)/(U_k_LV*LMTD_k_LV*Fk); % required heat transfer area in m2 at 
condenser (desuperheating)
    A_k = A_k_LV + A_dsh; % total heat transfer area of condenser in m2
    
    % Calculate ECONOMIC PARAMETERS
    % Estimate investment costs for the ORC power system
    % C_fixed is the total cost of the ORC power system in ÚSD
    C_fixed = computeCosts(mflow_wf,p_wf_eva,hout_wf_eva, p_wf_k, eta_p, eta_pm, 
eta_pm_hs, eta_pi, eta_pi_hs, eta_g, eta_f_cs, eta_pm_cs, eta_pi_cs, Tout_wf_p, T_wf_k, 
P_e, SP_e, n_e_stages, Medium, A_eva, A_k, A_rec, evaporatorType, pumpType, pumpType_hs, 
Tin_hs, P_p_hs, Dp_p_hs, mflow_hs, d_hs, P_f_cs, vflow_f_cs,xout_wf_eva,vflow_out_wf_e,
pumpType_cs,Dp_f_cs,vflow_out_wf_e_single);
   %[C_solar_fixed,output2] = Solar_indirect(I_D,Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,T_a, 
Tm,C_CL_Sp,C_Land_Sp,Land_req,cpm_hs,Tin_hs,Tout_hs_lim,dT_min_ST,t_st,L_CT_ini,L_HT_ini,
effectiveV,Q_in,Q_max,eta_g,P_e,eta_CL,Q_CL,A_CL,Tout_hs_eva);
   %[C_solar_fixed,output2] = Solar_direct(I_D,Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,T_a, Tm,
C_CL_Sp,C_Land_Sp,Land_req,cpm_hs,Tin_hs,Tout_hs_lim,dT_min_ST,t_st,L_CT_ini,L_HT_ini,
effectiveV,Q_in,Q_max,eta_g,P_e,eta_CL,Q_CL,A_CL,Tout_hs_eva);
   [C_solar_fixed,output2] = Solar_bedrock(I_D,Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,T_a, Tm,
C_CL_Sp,C_Land_Sp,Land_req,cpm_hs,Tin_hs,Tout_hs_lim,dT_min_ST,t_st,L_CT_ini,L_HT_ini,
effectiveV,Q_in,Q_max,eta_g,P_e,eta_CL,Q_CL,A_CL,Tout_hs_eva);
   
    if  isnan(C_fixed)
        if strcmp(goal, 'LCOE')
            z = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'A_CL')
            z = 1e15;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'SIC')
            z = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'NPV')
            z = 1e15;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'PBT')
            z = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal,'C_inv_tot')
            z = 1e15;   
        elseif strcmp(goal,'LCOW')
            z = 1e15; 
        end
        return
    end
    
    %% Costs 
    C_power_system=(1.18*C_fixed/1.2)+C_dest;  %Multiplication factor to take into 
account contingency and fee costs and USD to EUR? and multiplication factor from USD to 
EUR (1.2 conversion factor)
    %C_dest=0;

    C_inv_tot=C_power_system+C_solar_fixed; % Total investment cost in EUR
    
    IC = C_inv_tot; 
    OC = C_om*IC; % O&M cost per year
    El = (P_e-P_p-P_p_hs-P_f_cs-P_p_cogen)/1000*h_year; % Electricity produced in 
kWh/year
    Heat_year = Heat*h_year; %heat earnings in €/year 
    %Heat_year=0;
    
    % Evaluation of the investment
    i = interest;
    f = @(k) 1/((1+i).^k);
    NPV_v=ones(N_op,1); % Calculate NPV
    num = IC;
    
    for j = 1:N_op
        num = num + OC*f(j) - (El*E_price+Heat_year)*f(j); % Calculate NPV for every year
        NPV_v(j)=num;
    end
    
    NPV = NPV_v(N_op); % Net present value in EUR at the last year of investment (N_op)
    
    NPV_v = -NPV_v;
    [~,index]=min(abs(NPV_v)); % Determine pay-back time
    if NPV_v(index)>=0
        PBT_ub=index;
        PBT_lb=index-1;
    else PBT_lb=index;
        PBT_ub=index+1;
    end
    
    if index>=N_op
        PBT=1000;
    else
        PBT=abs(PBT_ub-NPV_v(PBT_ub)/(NPV_v(PBT_ub)-NPV_v(PBT_lb))); % Pay-back time in 
years
    end
    
    num_OC=0; % Calculate LCOE
    den=0;
    den_W=0;
    for j=1:N_op
        num_OC=num_OC+OC*f(j);
        den=den+El*f(j);
    
    % Calculate LCOFW   
        den_W=den_W+V_heat_cap_year*f(j);
        
    end
    
    %LCOE=(IC+num_OC)/den; % Levelized cost of electricity in €/kWh

%TLORC BEDROCK THERMINOL 55
     if Medium=="cyclopen"
    LCOE=0.177167856;
elseif Medium=="toluene"
    LCOE=0.177425306;
elseif Medium == "EBENZENE"
    LCOE=0.201191329;
elseif Medium=="MM"
    LCOE=0.18906519;
elseif Medium=="MDM"
    LCOE=0.207591127;
elseif Medium=="Chlorobenzene"
    LCOE=0.196750749;
elseif Medium=="heptane"
    LCOE=0.186690379;
elseif Medium=="hexane"
    LCOE=0.183495638;
    end
 
 
    LCOW=((IC+num_OC)-(LCOE*den))/(den_W); 
    SIC = IC/((P_e-P_p-P_p_hs-P_f_cs-P_p_cogen)/1000); % Specific investment cost of the 
whole plant in €/kWel
    C_power_system_sp=C_power_system/1.2/((P_e-P_p-P_p_hs-P_f_cs-P_p_cogen)/1000); % 
Total specific cost of power system in €/kWel
end
 
 
% return goal function for minimization
if strcmp(goal, 'LCOE')
    z = LCOE;
elseif strcmp(goal, 'A_CL')
    z = A_CL;
elseif strcmp(goal, 'SIC')
    z = C_power_system_sp;
elseif strcmp(goal, 'NPV')
    z = NPV;
elseif strcmp(goal, 'PBT')
    z = PBT;
elseif strcmp(goal,'C_inv_tot')
    z = C_inv_tot;   
elseif strcmp(goal,'LCOW')
    z = LCOW;     
end
end

function [output,costs_sp] = designFun_General(in, Tout_hs_lim, cpm_hs, Medium, eta_p_hs, 
eta_pm_hs, eta_pi_hs, Dp_p_hs, eta_f_cs, eta_pm_cs, eta_pi_cs, Dp_f_cs, eta_p, eta_pm, 
eta_g, p_wf_eva_max, p_wf_eva_min, Tout_wf_eva_max,xout_wf_eva_max, xout_wf_eva_min, 
dTpp_max, dTpp_min, Tin_cs, dTpp_k_max, dTpp_k_min, T_wf_k_max,T_wf_k_min,Q_max, goal, 
Tin_hs,eta_pi,n_e_stages,interest, N_op,h_year,C_om,MI,E_price, compCosts,P_eva_hs,P_e,
I_D,Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,T_a, Tm,C_CL_Sp,C_Land_Sp,Land_req,dT_min_ST,t_st,
L_CT_ini,L_HT_ini,effectiveV,Pen,Heat_price,Tc_wf)
 
% Input vector in absolute terms
p_wf_eva = (in(1)*(p_wf_eva_max-p_wf_eva_min)+p_wf_eva_min); % evaporation pressure of 
working fluid in kPa
xout_wf_eva = in(2)*(xout_wf_eva_max-xout_wf_eva_min)+xout_wf_eva_min; % evaporator 
outlet temperature of working fluid in K
rec_eff = in(3); % recuperator effectiveness
dTpp = in(4)*(dTpp_max-dTpp_min)+dTpp_min; % pinch-point temperature difference at 
evaporator in K
dTpp_k = in(5)*(dTpp_k_max-dTpp_k_min)+dTpp_k_min; % pinch-point temperature difference 
at condenser in K
T_wf_k = in(6)*(T_wf_k_max - T_wf_k_min)+T_wf_k_min; % condensing temperature in K
Tin_hs = min(Tin_hs) + in(7)*(min(Tin_hs) - min(Tin_hs)); % inlet temperature of heat 
source in K
p_wf_k = refpropm('P', 'T', T_wf_k, 'Q', 0, Medium); % condensing pressure in kPa
 
 
%% Cycle calculations
[h_wf_eva_L, T_wf_eva_L] = refpropm('HT','P',p_wf_eva,'Q',0,Medium); % temperature in K 
and specific enthalpy in J/kg at sat. liquid
[h_wf_eva_V, T_wf_eva_V] = refpropm('HT','P',p_wf_eva,'Q',1,Medium); % temperature in K 
and specific enthalpy in J/kg at sat. vapour
hout_wf_eva = h_wf_eva_L + xout_wf_eva*(h_wf_eva_V - h_wf_eva_L); % specific enthalpy in 
J/kg for working fluid at evaporator outlet
 
Tout_wf_eva = refpropm('T','H', hout_wf_eva, 'P',p_wf_eva, Medium); % temperature in K 
for working fluid at evaporator outlet
 
SHout_wf_eva = Tout_wf_eva - T_wf_eva_V; % degree of superheating at evaporator outlet 
for working fluid in K
sout_wf_eva = refpropm('S','H',hout_wf_eva,'P',p_wf_eva,Medium); % specific entropy at 
evaporator output for working fluid in J/kgK
 
[hin_wf_p, sin_wf_p] = refpropm('HS','T',T_wf_k,'Q',0,Medium); % specific enthalpy in 
J/kg and specific entropy in J/kgK of working fluid at pump inlet
hout_wf_ps = refpropm('H','P',p_wf_eva,'S',sin_wf_p,Medium); % specific enthalpy of 
working fluid at pump outlet in J/kg (isentropic)
hout_wf_p = hin_wf_p + (hout_wf_ps-hin_wf_p)/eta_p; % specific enthalpy of working fluid 
at pump outlet in J/kg
Tout_wf_p = refpropm('T','H',hout_wf_p,'P',p_wf_eva,Medium); % temperature of working 
fluid at pump outlet in K
 
%loop to iteratively calculate expander efficiency
%eta_e=0.8;

fluid at turbine outlet in J/kg

    qflow_rec = rec_eff*(hout_wf_e - refpropm('H', 'T', Tout_wf_p, 'P', p_wf_k, Medium));

temperature of working fluid leaving the recuperator in K (to condenser)

temperature of working fluid leaving the recuperator in K (to evaporator)
    hout_wf_rec_eva = hout_wf_p + qflow_rec;
    hout_wf_rec_k = hout_wf_e - qflow_rec;
    % temperature at recuperator outlet has to be single phase
    if Tout_wf_rec_eva > T_wf_eva_L - 5 || Tout_wf_rec_k < T_wf_k + 2
        if strcmp(goal, 'LCOE')
            LCOE = 1e5;
        elseif strcmp(goal, 'A_CL')

    Qflow_eva_intoL = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva - hout_wf_rec_eva); % preheating heat rate at 
evaporator in W

else % For PEORC with x>0 and SCORC architectures
    T_hs_eva_L=T_wf_eva_L+dTpp;
    mflow_hs = -mflow_wf*(-hout_wf_eva+h_wf_eva_L)/(cpm_hs*(Tin_hs-T_hs_eva_L));
    Qflow_eva_intoL = mflow_wf*(h_wf_eva_L - hout_wf_rec_eva); % preheating heat rate at

    Tout_hs_eva=T_hs_eva_L-Qflow_eva_intoL/(mflow_hs*cpm_hs);
    Qflow_LtoOut = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva-h_wf_eva_L); % heat rate from sat. L to outlet

    Qflow_eva_LV = mflow_wf*(min(h_wf_eva_V, hout_wf_eva) - h_wf_eva_L); % latent heat

    T_hs_eva_V=T_hs_eva_L + Qflow_eva_LV/(mflow_hs*cpm_hs); % temperature of heat source 
at evaporator sat. V in K
    if Tout_hs_eva-Tout_wf_rec_eva < dTpp % This means that the pinch point is at the

        Tout_hs_eva=Tout_wf_rec_eva +dTpp;
        mflow_hs = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva-hout_wf_rec_eva)/(cpm_hs*(Tin_hs-Tout_hs_eva));
        Qflow_eva_intoL = mflow_wf*(h_wf_eva_L - hout_wf_rec_eva); % preheating heat rate

        T_hs_eva_L=Tout_hs_eva+Qflow_eva_intoL/(mflow_hs*cpm_hs);
        Qflow_LtoOut = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_eva-h_wf_eva_L); % heat rate from sat. L to

        Qflow_eva_LV = mflow_wf*(min(h_wf_eva_V, hout_wf_eva) - h_wf_eva_L); % latent

        T_hs_eva_V=T_hs_eva_L + Qflow_eva_LV/(mflow_hs*cpm_hs); % temperature of heat

    end

% ORC working fluid pump
Vflow_p = mflow_wf/refpropm('D', 'T', Tout_wf_p, 'P', p_wf_eva, Medium); % volume flow 
rate at pump outlet in m3/s
head_p = (p_wf_eva-p_wf_k)*10^3/9.81/refpropm('D', 'T', Tout_wf_p, 'P', p_wf_eva, 
Medium); % pump head in m
Ns_p = 1800*sqrt(Vflow_p*3600/0.227125)/((head_p/0.3048)^0.75); % specific rotational 
speed of pump at 1800 rpm (US-Einheiten)
P_p = mflow_wf*(hout_wf_p - hin_wf_p)/eta_pm/eta_pi; % electric power for ORC pump in W
if Ns_p < 500
    pumpType = 'positive displacement';
else
    pumpType = 'centrifugal';
end

source pump in W
Vflow_p_hs = mflow_hs/d_hs; % volume flow rate at cold source at pump outlet in m3/s

rotational speed of pump at 1800 rpm (US-Einheiten)

% Cold source fan

%Cogeneration
 Q_heat_sp = 128; %Specific heat cost pr. m^3 in kWh_th / m3
 V_heat = (Qflow_k/1000) / Q_heat_sp %volume of heated water generated in m3
 V_heat_cap = (V_heat * 24*0.45) %m3/day (because Capacity_0 is given as m3/day)*capacity 
factor
 V_heat_cap_year = V_heat_cap * 365.25 %water volume generated pr year
 
%  
% % %electricity consumption for cogeneration
P_p_cogen_sp = 1.3;  %pump electricity consumption in kWh_th/m3
P_p_cogen_kWh = V_heat * P_p_cogen_sp %pump power in kWh

    % Determine heat transfer surface area of heat exchangers
    % Evaporator
    U_hs_eva = 1000; % RP:4000 heat transfer coefficient of heat source (geothermal 
fluid) in W/m2K at evaporator
    f_hs_eva = 0.00018; % fouling resistance of heat source (geothermal fluid) in m2K/W 
at evaporator
    A_ratio_hs_eva = 0.78; %RP:0.78 area ratio of heat source with respect to bare outer 
area at evaporator
    
    U_wf_ph = 1000; % RP:1000 heat transfer coefficient of working fluid in W/m2K at 
evaporator (preheating)
    U_wf_LV = 2200; % RP:2200 heat transfer coefficient of working fluid in W/m2K at 
evaporator (two-phase)
    U_wf_sh = 600; % RP: 1100 heat transfer coefficient of working fluid in W/m2K at 
evaporator (superheating)
    f_wf_eva = 0.00018; % fouling resistance of working fluid in m2K/W at evaporator

    A_ratio_wf_eva = 1; % area ratio of working fluid with respect to bare outer area at

    U_ph = 1/(1/(U_hs_eva*A_ratio_hs_eva) + f_hs_eva/A_ratio_hs_eva + 1/
(U_wf_ph*A_ratio_wf_eva) + f_wf_eva/A_ratio_wf_eva); % global heat transfer coefficient 
in W/m2K at evaporator (preheating)
    TD_HT_ph = T_hs_eva_L - min(Tout_wf_eva,T_wf_eva_L); % high temperature difference in

    TD_LT_ph = Tout_hs_eva - Tout_wf_rec_eva; % low temperature difference in K 
(counterflow) at evaporator (preheating)
    LMTD_ph = (TD_HT_ph - TD_LT_ph)/log(TD_HT_ph/TD_LT_ph); % logarithmic temperature 
difference in K(counterflow) at evaporator (preheating)
    A_ph = Qflow_eva_intoL/(U_ph*LMTD_ph); % required heat transfer area in m2 at 
evaporator (preheating)
    
    U_LV = 1/(1/(U_hs_eva*A_ratio_hs_eva) + f_hs_eva/A_ratio_hs_eva + 1/
(U_wf_LV*A_ratio_wf_eva) + f_wf_eva/A_ratio_wf_eva); % global heat transfer coefficient 
in W/m2K at evaporator (two-phase)
    TD_HT_LV = T_hs_eva_V - T_wf_eva_L; % high temperature difference in K(counterflow) 
at evaporator (two-phase)
    TD_LT_LV = T_hs_eva_L - T_wf_eva_L; % low temperature difference in K(counterflow) at 
evaporator (two-phase)
    
    if abs(TD_HT_LV/TD_LT_LV-1)<=1E-8
        LMTD_LV=0;
    else
        LMTD_LV = (TD_HT_LV - TD_LT_LV)/log(TD_HT_LV/TD_LT_LV); % logarithmic temperature 
difference in K(counterflow) at evaporator (two-phase)
    end

(U_wf_sh*A_ratio_wf_eva) + f_wf_eva/A_ratio_wf_eva); % global heat transfer coefficient

(counterflow) at evaporator (superheating)

        num = num + OC*f(j) - (El*E_price+Heat_year)*f(j); % Calculate NPV for every year

    NPV = NPV_v(N_op); % Net present value in EUR at the last year of investment (N_op)
    
    NPV_v = -NPV_v;
    [~,index]=min(abs(NPV_v)); % Determine pay-back time
    if NPV_v(index)>=0
        PBT_ub=index;
        PBT_lb=index-1;
    else PBT_lb=index;
        PBT_ub=index+1;
    end
    
    if index>=N_op || index<=1

function [C_solar_fixed,output2] = Solar_bedrock(I_D,Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,
T_a, Tm,C_CL_Sp,C_Land_Sp,Land_req,cpm_hs,Tin_hs,Tout_hs_lim,dT_min_ST,t_st,L_CT_ini,
L_HT_ini,effectiveV,Q_in,Q_max,eta_g,P_e,eta_CL,Q_CL,A_CL,Tout_hs_eva)
%% Solar field
 
%Area of solar field
A_field = A_CL * Land_req; %The total area required for the entire solar field in m^2 
(tror jeg)
 
%Cost for solar collectors
C_CL = A_CL * C_CL_Sp; %Total cost of solar collectors in €
C_A = A_field * C_Land_Sp; %Total cost of land area in €
C_solar_fixed = C_CL + C_A; %Total cost of the solar in €
 
%% Solar circuit
               % Therminol 55 data
               therminol_prop=readmatrix('therminol_55_T_rho_cp');
               Therminol_T=therminol_prop(:,1);
               Therminol_rho=therminol_prop(:,2);
               Therminol_cp=therminol_prop(:,3);
               cpm_hs=interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_cp,Tm,'spline','extrap');
 
%% Storage
T_H_i = Tin_hs; %Inlet temperature of the hot tank
T_C_i = Tout_hs_lim;  %Inlet temperature of the cold tank
 
 
Q_st_kWh = (Q_CL/1000)*t_st;    %Energy in storage after 8 hours in kWh
Q_st = (Q_CL/1000)*(t_st*3600); %Energy in storage after 8 hours in kJ
 
% % For Therminol 55:
Cp_F_ST=interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_cp,(T_H_i+T_C_i)/2,'spline','extrap')/1000; %
interpolated heat capacity of the fluid in the storage in kJ/(kg*K)
rho_F_ST =interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_rho,(T_H_i+T_C_i)/2,'spline','extrap'); %
interpolated density of the fluid in the storage in kg/m^3
 
% For rapeseed oil(Ref. J. F. Hoffmann, G. Vaitilingom, J. F. Henry, M.
% Chirtoc, R. Olives, V. Goetz, X. Py):
% T=(T_H_i+T_C_i)/2 - 273.15; %Cp correlation for rapeseed oil is in deg C
% Cp_F_ST = (1.621*10^(-9)*T^(4)-8.735* 10^(-7)*T^(3)+14.933*10^(-5)*T^(2)-5.976*10^(-3)
*T+2.0985); %heat capacity of rapeseed oil in kJ/(kg*K)
% rho_F_ST = 928.19-0.6691*T; %density for rapeseed oil in kg/m3
% % % % 
rho_S_ST = 2640; %density of the stone in kg/m^3 at mean temperature of the rock
Cp_S_ST = 1.050; %heat capacity of the rock in the storage in kJ/(kg*K)
 
epsilon = 0.22; % porosity of the packed-bed rock TES (gaps in between the stones)
T_max_ST = Tin_hs;
T_min_ST = Tout_hs_eva;
 
M_F_Total_req = (epsilon*rho_F_ST*Q_st)/ ((rho_F_ST*Cp_F_ST*epsilon+ rho_S_ST*Cp_S_ST*(1-
==PAGE:168==
28-06-21 16:23
C:\MATLAB\Solar_bedrock.m
2 of 2
epsilon)* (T_max_ST-T_min_ST))); %total mass required of fluid in kg
M_S_Total_req = ((1-epsilon)*rho_S_ST*Q_st)/ ((rho_F_ST*Cp_F_ST*epsilon+ 
rho_S_ST*Cp_S_ST*(1-epsilon)*(T_max_ST-T_min_ST))); %total mass required of rock in kg
 
%For-loop to iteratively calculate ...
H_T=1;
N=3; % orders - # of loops run %change to 20 or something when final
E=1e-6;
for j=1:N
 
D_T = 0.5 * H_T;
V_T = (pi/4)*D_T^2*H_T;
 
H_T = ((4 * Q_st) / (pi*(D_T/H_T)^2 * (rho_F_ST*Cp_S_ST*epsilon+ rho_S_ST*Cp_S_ST*(1-
epsilon))* (T_max_ST-T_min_ST)))^(1/3);
end
 
%% Cost of storage
C_salt_sp = 2.7; %cost of therminol 55 in €/kg
%C_salt_sp = 0.5; %cost of rapeseed oil in €/kg (Ref. J. F. Hoffmann, G. Vaitilingom, J. 
F. Henry, M. Chirtoc, R. Olives, V. Goetz, X. Py)
 
C_rock_sp = 0.5*1.113; %For 2016, Niedermeier et al. 2018"
C_therminol = C_salt_sp * M_F_Total_req; %Total cost of therminol 55/rapeseed oil in € 
C_rock = C_rock_sp * M_S_Total_req; %cost of rock in €
 
C_tank = (7932160*(V_T/30844)^0.85); %cost of storage tanks in € (Herrmann et al.)
C_FS_ST = C_rock+C_therminol; %cost of fluid and solid storage tanks
 
%C_ST_balance =C_ST*0.14       %cost of balance of TES including cost of storage pumps is 
14% of total cost of storage (Herrmann et al.)
%C_ST = C_salt+C_tank+C_ST_balance  %two equations two unknowns
%solved in maple to:
C_ST_balance=0.162790698*C_tank+0.162790698*C_FS_ST;
C_ST = C_FS_ST+C_tank+C_ST_balance;
    
%Cost of civil work
C_civil= 133*(P_e/1000) - 0.00042*(P_e/1000)^2; %cost of civil work in € (Krishnamurthy 
et al.)
C_misc = 144*(P_e/1000);
 
 
%% Extract results
[output2] = [eta_CL;Q_CL;A_CL;A_field;C_CL;C_A;C_ST;C_civil;C_misc;1;1;C_tank;
C_ST_balance;Q_st_kWh;Q_st];
[C_solar_fixed] = [C_CL+C_A+C_ST+C_civil+C_misc];%+price for working fluid in solar 
circuit and C_misc
 
end

function [C_solar_fixed,output2] = Solar_direct(I_D,Const_CL_A,Const_CL_B,Const_CL_C,T_a,

               % Therminol 55 data
               therminol_prop=readmatrix('therminol_55_T_rho_cp');

               Therminol_rho=therminol_prop(:,2);

T_H_ini = Tin_hs-30; %Initial hot storage temperature

Q_st = (Q_CL/1000)*(t_st*3600); %Energy in storage after 8 hours in kJ

density of the hot storage

density of the cold storage
 
rho_H_ini =interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_rho,T_H_ini,'spline','extrap'); %interpolated 
density of the hot storage
rho_C_ini =interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_rho,T_C_ini,'spline','extrap'); %interpolated 
density of the cold storage
 
Cp_H_ST=interp1(Therminol_T,Therminol_cp,(T_H_i+T_C_i)/2,'spline','extrap')/1000; %
interpolated heat capacity of the fluid in the storage in kJ/(kg*K)
 
 
% For rapeseed oil(Ref. J. F. Hoffmann, G. Vaitilingom, J. F. Henry, M.
% Chirtoc, R. Olives, V. Goetz, X. Py):
%  rho_H_ST= 928.19-0.6691*T_H_i;
%  rho_C_ST= 928.19-0.6691*T_C_i;

%  rho_C_ini = 928.19-0.6691*T_C_ini; %Initial density of the cold storage
%  
%  T=((T_H_i+T_C_i)/2) - 273.15; %temperature in celcius for rapeseed oil correlation
%  Cp_H_ST = (1.621*10^(-9)*T^(4)-8.735* 10^(-7)*T^(3)+14.933*10^(-5)*T^(2)-5.976*10^(-3)
*T+2.0985); %specific heat capacity of the storage for rapeseed oil

M_C = M_H; %Mass of the cold storage is equal to the mass of the hot storage

V_C_ST = M_C/(rho_C_ST*effectiveV); %volume of the cold water storage in m^3
 
D_H = (2*(V_H_ST*pi^2)^(1/3))/(pi); %diameter of the hot storage tank, assuming the 
height is 0.5 of the diameter
H_H = D_H*0.5; %height of the hot storage tank
 
D_C = (2*(V_C_ST*pi^2)^(1/3))/(pi); %diameter of the cold storage tank, assuming the 
height is 0.5 of the diameter
H_C = D_C*0.5; %height of the cold storage tank

mass requirement of the storage tanks
 
%% Cost of storage

%C_salt_sp = 0.5; %cost of rapeseed oil in €/kg (Ref. J. F. Hoffmann, G. Vaitilingom, J. 
F. Henry, M. Chirtoc, R. Olives, V. Goetz, X. Py)
C_salt = C_salt_sp * M_Total_req; %Total cost of therminol 55/rapeseed oil in € 
C_tank = (7932160*((V_H_ST+V_C_ST)/30844)^0.85); %cost of storage tanks in € (Herrmann et 
al.)
 
%C_ST_balance =C_ST*0.14                    %cost of balance of TES including cost of 
storage pumps is 14% of total cost of storage (Herrmann et al.)
%C_ST = C_salt+C_tank+C_ST_balance  %two equations two unknowns
%solved in maple to:
C_ST_balance=0.162790698*C_tank+0.162790698*C_salt;
C_ST = C_salt+C_tank+C_ST_balance;
    
%Cost of civil work
C_civil= 133*(P_e/1000) - 0.00042*(P_e/1000)^2; %cost of civil work in € (Krishnamurthy 
et al.)
C_misc = 144*(P_e/1000);
 
%% Extract results
[output2] = [eta_CL;Q_CL;A_CL;A_field;C_CL;C_A;C_ST;C_civil;C_misc;M_Total_req;C_salt;
C_tank;C_ST_balance;Q_st_kWh;Q_st];
[C_solar_fixed] = [C_CL+C_A+C_ST+C_civil+C_misc];%+price for working fluid in solar 
circuit and C_misc

function [eta_e, n_e_stages,vflow_out_wf_e_single] = turbeff(SP_e,VR_e,n_e_stages_dh,
xout_wf_eva,vflow_out_wf_e,mflow_wf,rho_out_wf_e,P_e,eta_g,rho_in_wf_e)
%% Expander turbine
%-if xout_wf_eva>=1 
  
%Fi and Ai values from "Axial flow turbines for Organic Rankine Cycle applications" page 
313
Fi = [1; log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)); (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^2; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; 
(log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^4; VR_e; log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)); (log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^2; (log
(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; (log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^4; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))*(log(VR_e)/log
(exp(1))); (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))*(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^2; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^2*
(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1))); (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))*(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; (log(SP_e)
/log(exp(1)))^2*(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^3*(log(VR_e)/log(exp
(1)))^2];
load('Ai_table')
Ai_tablest1 = Ai_table(:, [1]); %Ai values for 1 stage
Ai_tablest2 = Ai_table(:, [2]); %Ai values for 2 stages
Ai_tablest3 = Ai_table(:, [3]); %Ai values for 3 stages
 
%calculates the efficiency for 1, 2 and 3 stages using the corrletations
%from "Axial flow turbines for Organic Rankine Cycle applications" page 313
e_e1 = sum(Ai_tablest1.*Fi);
e_e2 = sum(Ai_tablest2.*Fi);
e_e3 = sum(Ai_tablest3.*Fi);
 
e_e=[e_e1,e_e2,e_e3];
 
 
%checks if its worth increasing number of stages
if (e_e3-e_e2)/e_e2 >= 0.03 && (e_e2-e_e1)/e_e1 >= 0.03 %if the efficiency increase with 
3% from 1-2 stages AND 2-3 stages, then use 3 stages
    e_eopt = e_e3;
elseif (e_e2-e_e1)/e_e1 >= 0.03 %if not 3% increase for both 1->2 and 2->3 stages, then 
check if 3% increase from 1-2 stages
    e_eopt = e_e2; %if 1->2 stages increase efficiency with 3% or more, use 2 stages
else
    e_eopt = e_e1; % if no significant efficiency increase, use only one stage
end
 
 
%check which efficiency is used and define the number of stages
if e_eopt == e_e3 
    n_e_stages = 3;    
elseif e_eopt == e_e2
    n_e_stages = 2;
elseif e_eopt == e_e1
    n_e_stages = 1;
end
 
n_e_stages=max(n_e_stages,n_e_stages_dh);
eta_e = e_e(min(n_e_stages,3)); %the efficiency of the expander, depending on number of 
==PAGE:175==
29-06-21 09:28
C:\MATLAB\turbeff.m
2 of 2
stages
 
%eta_e=0.8;
 
%for computational reasons:
vflow_out_wf_e_single = vflow_out_wf_e /n_e_stages;
 
%end

function [eta_e, n_e_stages,vflow_out_wf_e_single,n_e_stages_VR_limit,C_expander] = 
turbeff(SP_e,VR_e,n_e_stages_dh,xout_wf_eva,vflow_out_wf_e,mflow_wf,rho_out_wf_e,P_e,
eta_g,rho_in_wf_e)
%% Expander turbine
if xout_wf_eva>=1 
  
%Fi and Ai values from "Axial flow turbines for Organic Rankine Cycle applications" page 
313
Fi = [1; log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)); (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^2; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; 
(log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^4; VR_e; log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)); (log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^2; (log
(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; (log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^4; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))*(log(VR_e)/log
(exp(1))); (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))*(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^2; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^2*
(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1))); (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))*(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; (log(SP_e)
/log(exp(1)))^2*(log(VR_e)/log(exp(1)))^3; (log(SP_e)/log(exp(1)))^3*(log(VR_e)/log(exp
(1)))^2];
load('Ai_table')
Ai_tablest1 = Ai_table(:, [1]); %Ai values for 1 stage
Ai_tablest2 = Ai_table(:, [2]); %Ai values for 2 stages
Ai_tablest3 = Ai_table(:, [3]); %Ai values for 3 stages
 
%calculates the efficiency for 1, 2 and 3 stages using the corrletations
%from "Axial flow turbines for Organic Rankine Cycle applications" page 313
e_e1 = sum(Ai_tablest1.*Fi);
e_e2 = sum(Ai_tablest2.*Fi);
e_e3 = sum(Ai_tablest3.*Fi);
 
e_e=[e_e1,e_e2,e_e3];
 
 
%checks if its worth increasing number of stages
if (e_e3-e_e2)/e_e2 >= 0.03 && (e_e2-e_e1)/e_e1 >= 0.03 %if the efficiency increase with 
3% from 1-2 stages AND 2-3 stages, then use 3 stages
    e_eopt = e_e3;
elseif (e_e2-e_e1)/e_e1 >= 0.03 %if not 3% increase for both 1->2 and 2->3 stages, then 
check if 3% increase from 1-2 stages
    e_eopt = e_e2; %if 1->2 stages increase efficiency with 3% or more, use 2 stages
else
    e_eopt = e_e1; % if no significant efficiency increase, use only one stage
end
 
 
%check which efficiency is used and define the number of stages
if e_eopt == e_e3 
    n_e_stages = 3;    
elseif e_eopt == e_e2
    n_e_stages = 2;
elseif e_eopt == e_e1
    n_e_stages = 1;
end
 
n_e_stages=max(n_e_stages,n_e_stages_dh);
==PAGE:177==
29-06-21 09:38

2 of 9
eta_e = e_e(min(n_e_stages,3)); %the efficiency of the expander, depending on number of 
stages
 
%eta_e=0.77;
 
%for computational reasons:
vflow_out_wf_e_single = vflow_out_wf_e /n_e_stages;
VR_e_single = 1
n_e_stages_VR_limit = 1
C_expander = 1
 
end
%% PEORC architecture: two-phase screw expander (Astolfi PhD)
if xout_wf_eva<1
    
%for computational reasons:    
vflow_out_wf_e_single = 1
VR_e_single = 1
 
 
%     eta_e = 1; %for diagram
%     n_e_stages_VR_limit = ceil(VR_e/5) %number of stages from VR limit for parallel 
   n_e_stages_VR_limit = ceil(log(VR_e)/log(5)) %number of stages from VR limit for 
series
   
    VC=0.6 %volume coefficient in m3/MJ
    vflow_out_e_single_VC = (VC/(1*10^6))*(P_e/eta_g) %maximum volume flow pr expander 
m^3/J
     
    %Total VC 
    VC_tot = vflow_out_wf_e/((P_e/eta_g)/(1*10^6))
    
   A_0 = 0.9403305;
   A_1 = 0.0293295;
   A_2 =-0.0266298;
   
   if n_e_stages_VR_limit==2
   
   %first stage in series    
   VR_e_single_1 = 5    
   rho_out_wf_e_1 = rho_in_wf_e / VR_e_single_1
   vflow_out_wf_e_1 = mflow_wf / rho_out_wf_e_1
   eta_e_1 = A_0 + A_1*log(vflow_out_wf_e_1) + A_2*VR_e_single_1
 
   
   %second stage in series
   
   VR_e_single_2 = VR_e / 5
   rho_in_wf_e_2 = rho_out_wf_e_1
   rho_out_wf_e_2 = rho_in_wf_e_2 / VR_e_single_2
   vflow_out_wf_e_2 = mflow_wf / rho_out_wf_e_2
==PAGE:178==
